BUSHRANGING & OUTLAWRY
IN AUSTRALASIA.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
BY PRICE WARUNG.
totm Caesar's name must head the roll of
" r ppkless, maddened, or brutal spirits who
taken to the bush.” He was, as we have
tl'rlvou a transport landed from the First Fleet,
der sentence of seven years’ transportation. A
Indian negro, he was, in Judge Advocate
rUins’ opinion, “a mere animal,” but animal or
I e W asadmitted by the sameauthority to possess
D °: a j one a frame suitable for hard labour, but
To the willingness to labour. “Ho was reputed
f hardest working convict in the country . .
Tit in his intellects he did not differ very widely
r ora a brute.” And—here is part of the explana-
. pf his subsequent career—“ his appetite was
fvenous, for he could in any one day devour a
Ml ration for two days.” Part, we say ; and the
uher part is supplied by Michael Robinson, of
3 ,, m we have already spoken. That indifferent
let but excellent artist in perjury and forgery—
r f ]i e d very generally regretted, and the Sydney
PiZETTE put a mourning border round the notice
f his death—tells us that the black Caesar’s food
3 frequently stolen from him by other persons
IJiio had whiter skins. That was very likely to be
.1 case . The weekly “male”ration at the time of
pesaV's first defiance of the Colonial Regulations
IVis the most liberal for several years. It con-
fisted of 71b. of biscuit, 11b. of flour, 71b. of beef,
nv4!b. of pork, 3 pints of pease, and 6oz. of
bittter—all things considered, not a stinted bill-of-
fufij But when the butter was rancid, and an
Mg ail d intelligent contractor had supplied bis-
cuits and flour which were weevilly before the
ressel which bore Caesar and his fortunes had left
the Downs, and the pease had a trick of vanishing
mysteriously from the cooking-pots, then, by the
time the “ white persons ’’—doubtless a commis-
sariat official or two—had helped themselves to the
perquisites of office, the chances were that Caesar’s
portion would not have satisfied the hunger of a
delicate damsel with the appetite of a nightingale.
4nd as though the rogues of the Transport de-
jnrtraeOtand the rascals of the local Commissariat
tvere Hot enough, Caesar, says Michael Robinson,
“was also shunned by Ids fellow-convicts, who
disliked him, being a man of colour.” The easy-
principled masters and madams upon whom the
editor of Phillip’s “Voyage to Botany Bay ” has
conferred an immortality which must excite the
envy of many a distinguished politician-strange
that Sir Bernard Burke did not reprint the
muster-list as an appropriate introduction to his
“Colonial Gentry ”!- were of a lofty temper
indeed when they found—
... a fellow guilty of a skin
Not coloured like their own.
Although England had exiled them, they were
still Englishmen in theory and practice, and Mew
South Wales, it was pretty well understood, was
to be “a New Britannia.” Therefore, as every-
body knew that “no slave could live in Britain,”
so neither could he exist in this newer Britain of
the South. Being a West Indian nigger, of
course, Black Caesar was no better than a slave;
and so our founders gave a new reading to the
maxim, “No slave could exist!” No, of course
not! There was no reason why he should. And,
accordingly, his rations were stolen. So Michael
Robinson insinuates, and being a lawyer, Robin-
son had no objection to suggesting the truth in
cases where there was nothing to be gained by
falsehood.
» # *
Life in the early years of Australia was
simply a struggle to fill the stomach. It was a
famine epoch. Under ordinary circumstances,
therefore, the food went to the strongest. The
weak, on the issue of their supply of rations for
the ensuing seven days, would often gorge as
much as they could to save it from the thieves.
One wretch made his whole week’s allowance of
flour and biscuit (eight pounds) into 18 cakes.
These were devoured at a single meal. Asa
method of saving a ration, this was not the way,
however, to commend itself to others, for he died,
the following day in the hospital. Some economies
are false.
Now, Csesar, being possessed of great muscular
strength, should, no doubt, have been able to take
care that whoever went empty and hungry he
would not. But his colour thrust him among the
weakly class. He was beaten and thrust out by
the strong: “they would not hutt the black.”
There was a fine freedom, and an admirable
absence of squeamishness in Earliest Sydney : the
transports obtained hut-room where they could—
that is, the men; and as for the women, it is
pleasing to record that there was never any
difficulty in.accommodating them—were there not
the officials’ and soldiers’ quarters at their com-
mand? For Caesar, however, there was no room
among the strong; and as for his stealing from
the weak, why there was not much profit in that
game, when the Commissariat had completed
what the wealthy English contractors had begun.
So—there being as yet no world of polities in
which he could display his powers, and no oppor-
tunity for land-syndicating, he took to the sys-
tematic theft of provender in order to live.
*■ # #
As we have already told you, he was ordered
in February, 1788, as a punishment for his first
offence, to be exiled “to South Cape.” But he
got no farther than the island in the habour,
called by the natives, Mat-te-wan-yee, by Collins,
Rock Island, by the convicts, Pinchgut, and now
known as Fort Denison. Hence, for the period
of some four months, Caesar existed with two
other offenders, the first victims of a practice
which obtains in Sydney to-day : the substitution
of “ magnificent harbour views ” for a substantial
“ table.” Some men build better than they
know, and Governor Phillip little thought in
banishing heinous offenders to Mat-te-wan-ye that
he was giving a hint to countless Sydney boarding-
house keepers of the following century.
On the 4th June, King George the Third’s birth-
day was celebrated. Phillip, in recognition of the
fact that His Gracious Majesty was one year
nearer lunacy and the grave, forgave Ceesar : and
retaking him on the mainland, assigned him to an
officer as a servant. A brief term of service, and
ho formally inaugurated the local industry of
bushranging which has employed so many hands
and been the means of circulating so much money.
Michael Robinson comments, “No wonder he dis-
appeared,” but he doesn’t state the reason for this
nil admirari view. Was it because the officer was
not a sufficiently moral associate for Cresar that
the latter bolted ? We shouldn’t be surprised.
Even a brute nigger might feel a qualm of con-
science as to intimacy with, say, Thomas Davey
of the Marines who instituted on Sydney Cove the
harem he maintained in Van Demonia later.
Anyhow, Caesar went, and with him vanished “ a
musket, an iron pot, and some provisions.” Re-
captured just when the stores were full again of
provender (in May, ’B9), ho was sent to Garden
Island in irons. Another four months he endured
of captivity and short commons, and again he
escaped, this time with a canoe, as well as a
musket and some ammunition. And now he be-
came a “ perfect pest ”to the infant community.
* # *
“ In vain,” writes Michael Robinson, “the
Governor dispatched forces in search.” In vain,
rewards were offered for his capture; in vain
decoys were set. At least, two women were
allowed to wander at large in the hope of attract-
ing him by their coy advances to their arms and
the new log prison. He kept out of the way of
the Marines, and the sirens lost themselves “in
the woods ” in their attempt to lure from the paths
of chastity the redoubtable black. With respect
to this latter expedient we must not be too severe
in our judgment on Governor Phillip. Himself a
Puritan, he was man of the world enough to
employ the acts of women as instruments to con-
trol dissolute men. This use of decoy women was,
according to his lights,,no more to be reprobated
than his idea that women should bo .furnished to
the Settlement from the South Sea Islands. This
generation cannot afford to blame Phillip when it
pensions the men who set traps for the latter-day
’rangers with ladies of easy virtue.
The Governor honoured Ctesar with a “General
Order” or two. And he made him the subject of
“Patrole” and “Counter - sign.” You could
generally tell when one of the early Governors
had something on his mind, by the allusion he
made to the “ something ”in fixing the signs and
words for the day. Whenever, for instance, a
convict having spent twenty, or thirty, or forty
years in proving himself unfit to live, received
twenty-four or forty-eight hours’ notice to make
himself fit to die, the “parole” would be usually
“ Justice,” and the c.s. “Death.” When a visit
to Parramatta was projected, it would be “Rose-
hill ” and “ Visitwhen the weather was fine, it
would be “Beautiful” and “Day;” when rain
fell, “ Seasonable ”or “ Unseasonable,’’ and
“Rain;” a threatened mutiny would strain the
gubernatorial imagination after its suppression
to the point of invention —“ Revolution ” and
“Crushed.” And so it is pretty plain, from his
orders for the day, what was uppermost in the
great man’s thoughts. Thus we know that when
“ Caesar” is linked in the “orders” with “Reward”
and “ Robberies,” he was a thorn in Phillip’s side.
# # #
He descended almost nightly upon the
settlement. Now it was a garden-plot at Gai'den
Island which was robbed of its vegetables ; now it
was one of the outlying huts that was favoured
with a predatory visit. He “must have had
comrades,” says Robinson, and no doubt he had.
In a community deprived of almost all topics of
talk except their own petty doings, the escapades
of a bold and venturesome spirit such as Caesar
was, were as springs in a desert land to thirsty
souls. “ They talked about him, and was fervently
hoped by many that he would not be taken.”
There can be little question that, in the minds of
the ignorant convicts and soldiers, the impression
made to his prejudice hy his colour had been dis-
sipated by his exploits. “ Even those who lost by
his thefts, if the articles stolen were from the
stores”—that is, if they were really Government
property on loan—“ were not so much grieved as
might be expected.” Well, the race of peopli
who can bear with equanimity the loss of what id
not theirs, has not yet died out.
* * *
The marines couldn’t shoot him. But
Robinson suggests they did not try : for the
offered reward—“partial remission of sentence
and an extra ration ” —did not attract them. They
had no use for the remission, and a ration they
could always steal. Yet he was seen several
times. The Governor and the Lieut.-Governor,
Major Ross, in the early period, and Lieutenant
Macarthur in the later, led expeditions in person.
We can almost imagine, as we note this, that we
are again in the epoch of the Gardiner and the
Kelly hunt when in the pursuit of the freebooters
the course of justice seemed arrested. Caesar was
the Dora Dora black of the 18th century. And,
as in the case of these twin imps of mischief of
whom we gain occasional dissolving views, it is
pretty certain ho was “seen” in many places
where he was not, and was accused of performing
many feats of which he was innocent. At the
same time, curiously enough, no true allegation of
murder was made against him. The most serious
offence laid to his charge was the breaking- open
of the King’s store. This the authorities regarded,
and properly regarded, as belonging to the cate-
gory of diabolical crimes, for at the time it occurred
food was again short. In April, 1790, an adult’s
rations was 241 b. flour, 2 of rice, and 2 of salt-pork.
This was reduced later; 1791 was one long hunger.
In 1792, however, there was a spasm of luxury;
in the beginning of the year a man got 51b. of flour
and 4lb. of pork ; in July he could revel in 41b.
maize, 3 sugar, 7 beef, 4 pint rice, 3 pints pease.
But next year witnessed a resumption of the
terror. There was no flour; “gram” (a horse-
grain) was supplied instead ; there were no proper
means of grinding. The wretched mills worked
all night, and often when the morning drum beat
for labour to be resumed, its roll sounded in the
ears of the starving, faintinar convicts who had
been waiting since nightfall for their turn at the
mills. In ’94 all provisions tvere issued from the
store six hours before the ship with expected sup-
plies was sighted. Suppose this vessel, the
Williams, had met with the fate of the Guardian,
and the growing maize-crop, as it had in previous
years, proved a failure, the experiment of the
colonisation of New South Wales might have
then and there come to a frightful end. The
awful mortality on board the*Second Fleet, which
should have stirred sympathy in the heart of a
stone statue, or of an officer of the New South
Wales Corps, was actually the theme of a degree
of rejoicing. It lessened the demands on the
stores. With these famine conditions to face-
conditions for which English statesmen, the
Pitts, and the Grenvilles, and the Sydneys, were
alone responsible—it was the duty of the early
Governors to punish rigorously any attempt to
rob the stores. Every ration thus stolen might,
perhaps, mean a life. Theft of food rvas really a
form of murder.
# » •
There was no actual evidence to convict
Caesar of the store robbery. The sentinel on duty
was arrested and acquitted, and suspicion fell
upon Caesar (as Robinson thinks), justly so. He
says “the black was cunning!” He must have
been more than cunning : he must have been sur-
prisingly bold and audacious to have attacked a
-store-house guarded by military and situated in a
street regularly patrolled. If he were a brute, he
was certainly a courageous one : to encounter the

f
loneliness “of the woods,” the risk of being
“ stung by serpents,” the horrors of contact with
the “savages.” “Caesar,” writes Robinson, “did
not join the savages as many expected.” Perhaps
Caesar, being only of their hue of skin, did not
impress the natives favourably. Lacking white-
ness, he lacked superiority. Had they welcomed
him, doubtless with his cunning and his fearless-
ness, his musket and his canoe, he would have
captained them in many an assault. But the
reception they gave him was not encouraging.
They speared him.
* * #
He had found his way from the Cove to
the younger settlement at Rosehill. There, after
bothering the authorities for a time, he gave him-
self up, wounded by the aborigines. In mitiga-
tion of punishment of his offences, he pleaded
that the wounds had been received in the service
of the infant State. The State had been greatly
embarrassed by the loss of the cattle. They had
been allowed to stray, and when wanted could not
be found. Now Caesar said he had seen the errant
herd being watched by aborigines. Then, he
averred, he drove the natives away, and was re-
turning to the settlement with the stock when he
was attacked and wounded by another party of
the * ‘ savages. ” Nobody believed his story, except
the portion as to his being wounded. “That,”
remarked Collins, “could not well be contradicted
as he had several spear-wounds about him.” As
to the rest, “he was well-known to have as small
a share of veracity as of honesty. ” Evidently, he
had the makings in him of a “brilliant financier,”
had Caesar !
* * *
This was in 1790. The authorities placed
him in hospital to recover —probably with a view
to hanging him as soon as he was well.' Ungrate-
ful, however, for this solicitude he once more
escaped, and for the next six years he led the
Government and the colonists, both at the Cove
and at Rosehill a merry dance. Now, it was the
store, now it was Garden Island, now it was the
huts and passers-by in the one sheet at Rosehill,
which suffered. He would patronise the Cove
one day, and while the town guards were beating
the woods for him, would visit Rosehill the next.
And so he went on, stealing food and clothes,
muskets (on occasions) and ammunition, for six
years. He was recaptured twice or thrice, laughed
in the authorities’ faces when they threatened to
hang him, was ironed —and escaped when he
pleased. He had followers, and crimes he did not
commit were laid to his charge, but with the
allowances for these performances, his own deeds
were, the sparse opportunities being taken into
account, as heinous as those of later ruffians.
# # *
At last the end came. On Monday, 15th
February, 1796, the population of Sydney enjoyed
a double"sensation. A soldier and a convict were
put upon their trial for burglary at the house of
the Rev. M>. Johnson, the Chaplain. The case
ended in acquittal, but probably- the people did
not object to the singularity of the issue, in their
senation at the news which came to hand while
the court was sitting. Cajsar had been shot!
# * #
The reward for his apprehension having
oeen largely increased, one Winbow and a comrade
resolved to earn it. They set out in search, and
remained “in the woods”'for several days, near
Liberty Plains, where ho was supposed to have
iris lair —in former years, says Robinson, “ t was
a cave on the North Shore.” At dusk one evening
the pursuers saw Caesar enter some brushwood.
They remained on watch all night. On his
emerging from shelter in the morning he saw his
danger, “and presented his musket; but before
he could pull the trigger, Winbow fired and shot
him.” Then he was taken to the hut of Rose or
Ruse —Collins says “ Rose,” but as, according to
Robinson, Ruse was the man who accompanied
Winbow, it may have been his hut. If it were
Ruse’s hut, the first bushranger died on the site
of the present Rook wood cemetery. Ruse had
many distinctions. He was the first man to turn
a plough-furrow in Australia; and the first freed
man to receive a grant of land, and now he assisted
at the death of the first bushranger’. For, “ having
given,” as Collins writes, “ more trouble than any
other convict in the settlement,” John Caesar
passed to his account. A terror to an infant
empire in life, he was a bogey to infant children
in death. The nurses of children born in the
early decades called on his shade regularly when
the youngsters were naughty. Thus he proved of
use when dead—and, after all, that cannot be said
of everyone.
The Men With Whom I Used to Shear.
A Ballade of Lost Mates.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Yes ! they are lost, why advertise !
For some can’t read, and some, I ween,
May now have pens up in the skies,
At least—if sheds up there are seen.
Ah ! some were straight and some were mean,
And some are blacklegs now, I fear—
Mad Harry, Smithy, Old Bill Deane,
The men with whom I used to shear.
Mick Ford could sing a rattling song,
And always swore his yarns were true ;
He went to dig at Adelong,
I don’t know what he couldn’t do,
His brother made good tallies, too;
From him I sometimes used to hear.
But, Lord ! they were a motley crew,
The men with whom I used to shear.
I often think of “ Parson ” Jack,
For five whole years none heard him.swear,
They used to say he got the sack
For drinking more than one man’s share.
Howbeit, we always found him square;
But when he preached they used to jeer,
A cent for hell they didn’t care,
The men with whom I used to shear.
Where's Dick the Swell, I wonder, now ?
The fellows had a set on him.
When we cut out he had a row
And very nearly killed Long Jim—
It made him feared at Cooranim
In sixty-nine (’twas his last year) ;
They’d filled his cup up to the brim,
The men with whom I used to shear.
Where are they all ? I’d like to know—-
In Sydney I can’t find a mate,
I'll go out West beyond Dubbo,
And find a shed before it’s late,
An >ther day I shall not wait,
I’ll never make a cheque down here,
And I may meet by Mogong gate,
The men with whom I used to shear.
Lane Cove.
A.C. FONTAING.
Restitution.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Brother Prator was an active man in Geelong
temperance circles3o years ago, and an earnest ex-
pounder of the Gospel in “ the little corner of the
Lord’s vineyard,” where the Cornish Wesleyans
assembled. What pious Cousih Jack of that time
and place has forgotten Silas Prater’s passionate
exhortation to the faithful: “Throw open the
skylights, brotherin, an’ let the speerit come in !” ?
Brother Prator was a short, heavy, bilious man
with sandy hair and thin, discouraged-looking
whiskers. His unwholesome skin was blotched
with peculiar bright yellow freckles, his eyes
lacked the sparkle of life and the power of concen-
tration, and were no more expressive than unripe
grapes. But, uninviting as he was in appearance,
Silas was fervent in the cause and strong for
righteousness. He never engaged in prayer for
the general benefit at the iron chapel, without
finishing, in a paroxysm of tears, with his face in
the dust upon the floor. There was no dissimula-
tion in all this ; Prator’s religious ecstacies were
genuine, and yet he was not superior to those
little tricks of trade to which more carnal-minded
tradesmen resort to swell their profits. He had
been caught using light weights at his humble
store, and it was well known amongst the elect
that he coveted his neighbour’s wife.
Prator was Best’s rival for the hand of the young
woman who eventually became Mrs. Best, and the
wedding was accepted amongst the congregation
as a great blow to their respected and energetic
superintendent. Certainly he became more morose
in his manner, and more hysterical in his religion
after that event, and assuredly he always displayed
a marked, if gloomy, appreciation of Mrs. Best’s
comeliness and of the pleasures of her society.
After a few years of married life Best died and
was buried, and it was noticed with sorrow and
righteous indignation by the “flock” that Silas
was pointedly courting the widow whilst her
weeds were yet, as it were, green upon her. But
the lady respected the conventionalities, and did
not yield to Brother Prator’s importunities till she
had complied with the demands of middle-class
respectability, and remained twelve months a
mourner. Then it was given out that she and
Silas were engaged, and three months later they
were wedded at the little corner of the vineyard.
His marriage for some time made little differ-
ence in Mr. Prator, or in his mode of life, but
by-and-bye the flock detected a curious change in
the couple. Silas’ natural gloom intensified into
obvious hypochondria—he rarely spoke to any of
the “brotherin” or “sisterin,” and he became
more furtive in his goings and comings. There
was evidently some heavy burden upon his mind.
Mrs. Prator, too, lost the gaiety that had formerly
characterised her, and was always sad and de-
pressed. The flock assured itself that she Was
terribly afraid of her second husband, and noticed
that she avoided him as much as possible, It was
hinted that he used her badly, but she never com-
plained.
There was a mystery, and the congregation at
the chapel troubled itself a great deal about the
matter. It whispered, and speculated, and
watched, but discovered nothing satisfying, till
Brother Prator opened his heart in prayer one
auspicious Sabbath morning, and exposed his
secret in one of his devotional frenzies. He was
beset by an awful vision ; nightly the spirit of
Brother Best visited his bedside, ahd P TTj
him in terrible tones to give up his vVife i U I%
believed that the spectre Was sent in • P ra ttj t

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 17, 1892.
“The Bulletin” and the "Respectable”
Press.
The judgment of New South Wales Supreme
Court with respect to the application of Mr.
Francis Abigail that the managing director
of The Bulletin should be put in gaol for
an alleged contempt of Court presents various
features deserving of particular attention.
We need do no more than refer with a smile
to the peculiar position in which the Court
itself stood in relation to this affair. That it
moved in the matter at all was due solely to
the delicate and sensitive regard of Mr.
Abigail for its dignity. It was Mr. Abigail’s
fine feelings which were lacerated by the
idea that The Bulletin had treated the
Supreme Court with contempt. The Court,
although it possesses the power of initiative
when so affronted, had not appeared con-
scious that anything had happened to it.
But the soul of Mr. Francis Abigail was
vexed at this lack of self-respect. With an
elevation of soul transcending that of the
Judges themselves he, in effect, rallied them
on their callousness, took their honour, as it
were, under his protection, and demanded
that they should vindicate their dignity. The
judgment of the Court, arrived at after
taking ample time for deliberation and
reflection, is in effect that Mr. Abigail’s
solicitude has been uncalled for and mis-
directed. Uncalled for, because there has
been no contempt ; misdirected, because,
although The Bulletin is guiltless as regards
the Court, it has been, in the opinion of the
Judges, reprohensibly cruel towards Abigail.
From this decision it must appear that the
Court is of opinion that Mr. Abigail has
endeavoured to avenge a private and personal
injury by unwarrantable means—that is to
say, by representing that it was the course
of Justice which had been impugned, whereas
the Judges declare that it was only the
course of Abigail.
• • •
The Bulletin, of course, repudiates the
alternative allegation, so far as any excep-
tional fault on its part is concerned. W hile
not claiming any immunity which is not
enjoyed by the non-illustrated journals, it
does claim equal rights with them. It main-
tains that whatever may bo publicly printed
in words may bo publicly printed in pictures
For example, if one newspaper is at
liberty to say that an accused person has
been sent to gaol, another newspaper has a
right to depict him being conveyed to the
gaol-door. It cannot but be most damaging
to any man to have it recorded, day after
day, that he has been brought up in custody,
or has appeared to answer to a charge, that
a magistrate was particular about the sub-
stantial amount of bail, that a man spent
the night in a lock-up cell, and so forth, as
continually is recorded concerning multitudes
of persons. And when newspapers begin by
respectfully styling the person arraigned
Mr. So-and-so, and presently refer to him
curtly as So-and-so, without the “Mister,” or
simply as “ the accused,” or “the prisoner,”
there can be no doubt that an effect pre-
judicial to him is made on the public mind.
There is just as sinister a significance in that
curtailment of respectful designation, as there
can be in any grotesqueness of illustrating
the incidents of the case. Scientifically con-
sidered, writing or printing are but highly-
developed hieroglyphs, while drawing, paint-
ing and sculpture are likewise hieroglyphs,
but in a less developed stage. If it should
happen, for example, that a public man of
whom a marble statue had been erected fell
under a charge of criminal offence, it would
be quite possible, by a few cuts of the chisel,
to so alter his aspect in the effigy as to create
an impression unfavourable to him. It is
recorded of Michael Angelo that with a
single incision on a finished statue he so
changed the expression of the placid face as
to impart to it a mocking diablerie. The
most remarkable distinction between the
hieroglyph which is writing or print, and the
hieroglyph which is drawing, is that infinitely
more meaning can be conveyed by one stroke
of the latter than can by the former process
be communicated without an infinity of
forms. All great impostors have dreaded
the pencil prodigiously more than they
have feared the pen “Boss Tweed
sustained for years without flinching the
unceasing and unrelenting exposures of some
of the most powerful journals of New York,
but he winced and quailed and succumbed
under the cartoons of Nast.
» • • .
With particular passages of the judgment
delivered by Chief-Justice Barley we are
substantially in accord. “Every publication
which may tend to interfere with the due
course of justice, or (is) made with a view of
influencing the ultimate result, is a contempt
of Court.” Again: “There is no doubt that
any publication, whether pictorial or other-
wise, affecting any person before the Court,
whether as a suitor or committed to take his
trial upon a criminal charge, and which
publication is calculated to interfere with
the due course of justice, or intended to pre-
judice the public against or in favour of the
suitor or person committed, is a serious crim-
inal offence.” We go further, and affirm that
the offence is serious if committed before the
person comes under the cognisance of the
higher tribunals. A person’s cause may be
deeply affected by comment published while
he is under magisterial examination. This
brings us to a distinction sought’ to be in-
sinuated in the course of the judgment that
there is a “law-abiding” section of the
press which would unanimously denounce
and repudiate anything of that kind—a
section to which The Bulletin does not
belong. The Bulletin needs to be in-
different to any insinuations of this kind on
the part of persons highly placed. One of
its strongest holds on public confidence has
been that it is no respecter of persons or
stations, and that it has ever tried to deal
more gently with the lowly, who most need
consideration, than with the mighty, whose
station and influence secure for them
compliances and deferences, and general
obsequiousness, w hich are so often rewarded
by appropriate compliments and complais-
ances. But the facts are contrary to
what the Judges in this deliverance have
apparently sought to convey. While The
Bulletin has never hesitated to satirise the
foibles of Governors, Judges and Ministers,
millionaires and magnates, it has persistently
exerted its influence to secure for persons in
humble station and devoid of powerful
friends or faithful toadies, that fairness of
treatment which the Courts have constantly
neglected to demand for such as they. The
Judges are under an obligation, now that
they have formulated and declared their just
views, to lower the level of their vision and
comprehend within it the man of humble
station as well as the man with pretensions
to elevated position. When they shall have
accomplished this alteration of focus they
will perceive that what they designate the
“ respectable press ” has been a persistent
and gross offender against the very canons
any breach of which, their Honors affirm
in this judgment, that section will “ denounce
and repudiate.”
# • #
The cfyse of Deeming is scarcely quite in
point, since the trial of that murderous
lunatic took place beyond the jurisdiction of
the New South Wales Supreme Court—but
noverthqkss in a province the “respectable”
press wlmreof has ever, in the case of obscure
persons charged with criminal offences, acted
with the same callous injustice as has per-
sistently characterised the capitalistic press
of New South Wales under like conditions.
Yet the Deeming case serves to exemplify
the universality of the rule that ignoble and
unfriended accused persons have been ex-
posed to the extremity of defamation while
their trials were in suspense. But there
have been instances enough—apart from the
atrocious and malignant savagery with which
the Sydney daily press treated the wretched
Mount Rennie prisoners—in New South
Wales under the very noses of the Judges
who now rouse themselves to deliver a judg-
ment so just in its essentials and so impres-
sive in its form. The unhappy wretch,
Schmidt, while under committal on a charge
of murder near Wagga not many months
ago, was the theme of continuous dam-
natory comment by almost every one of
the papers which seem to be indicated
by the Judges as incapable of such conduct.
Not only in endless accusatory allegations
of facts, but in pictorial representations,
several of the “respectable ” papers ( i.e ., the
journals which are openly react by persons
deriving large incomes from the courts)
hounded that untried man to his doom.
Wood-cuts, alleged to represent his features,
were published in more than one newspaper,
which could not all be likenesses since no
one resembled any other save in one particu-
lar, namely that each represented a counten-
ance so forbidding and beastlike that its
appearance conveyed an assurance of guilt.
In Deeming’s case, comment went into
particulars, and the public was informed that
those who had described him, as many of his
acquaintances did at first, as of agreeable and
even refined appearance, could not have
been competent to judge, inasmuch as his
aspect was saturnine and malicious. No
instance exists in our memory of a Court ever
having intervened to quell the licentious com-
ments of daily journals respecting miserable
prisoners, shut up in gaols for lack of
bail, and unprovided with the money to hire
lawyers to invoke on their behalf the inter-
vention of the Court to protect them from
the reckless remarks of the ‘ ‘ respectable
press,” On the whole, The Bulletin stands
guiltless of the cowardly criminality which,
while “respectably” abstaining from aught
that might by inconceivable chance
strengthen the prospects of the conviction of
an influential accused, relentlessly published
every rumour, every conjecture which could
prejudice the case of the unfriended and
destitute “ suspect.” As for the immediate
cause of the Judges’ deliberations in the
present instance, The Bulletin, desires for
Mr. Francis Abigail and his fellow-accuses
nothing but absolute justice.
Industrial Life Assurance.
Last week, in New South Wales Assembly,
the question of industrial life assurance came
up for discussion, and was solemnly con-
sidered by a number of legislators who were
almost all profoundly ignorant of the subject,
and who looked considerably wiser than usual
because of their ignorance. The matter was
introduced by Mr. Gary an, M.L.A. and
manager of the Citizens’ Life Assurance
Company, of Sydney, a proprietary institu-
tion which has branches in various Australian
provinces, and has of late come in for some
hostile criticism ; and that gentleman, for
some reason not very definable, stopped the
business of the country in order to read the
complete report and balance-sheet of his
company, to go into elaborate details about
the new actuary, the reserve fund, the
reversionary bonus, the “ English Life
Table, No. Ill,” the “loading,” the “net
liability,” the recent “quinquennial investi-
gation,” the average mortality, and other
matters, and generally to give his office a
large and comprehensive advertisement.
Why any man should be permitted to use
the Legislature as a means of booming his
insurance business, any more than he should
be allowed to move the adjournment of the
House for the purpose of drawing attention
to a hair-restorer upon commission, is not
very clear ; but Mr. Gary AN had at least the
satisfaction of utterly refuting his critics,
principally because none of them were suffi-
ciently acquainted with the business to
recognise the points which required criticism,
# # #
The principal accusations brought against
the Citizens’ Company were practically as
follows : That it has an annual premium
income of £lOl,OOO and spends £68,000 in
collecting this amount and in office expenses,
&c., leaving about £33,000 worth of actual life-
insurance for its policy-holders ; and that it
has collected, since its establishment five
years ago, about £270,000, of which amount
it has actually saved only £BO,OO0 —in other
words, every £1 it has saved has involved an
outlay of more than £2 to effect that saving.
The charge which excited the most acri-
monious criticism—that the company had
inflated its assets by suddenly adding £24,000
to the paper value of'its Sydney property —
is of little account. A merciful Providence
placed the Citizens’ Co. ’s office at the corner
of Moore-street, a new thoroughfare recently
created at a large outlay of public money,
and for want of a thoroughly efficient better-
ment law the company has profited thereby,
possibly tt=> an even greater extent than it
has taken credit for.
# # *
Even the extraordinary figures in the com-
pany’s balance-sheet are in themselves no
evidence that it is unsound ; in fact, there
appears to be every reason to believe that it
is sound —at present. If such a balance-
sheet wore issued by the A.M.P. or the
Colonial Mutual or any other society doing
the ordinary description of insurance busi-
ness, it would mean utter ruin, desolation
and insolvency. But industrial assurance —
on which the Citizens’ Co. chiefly depends—
is an entirely different class of business from
that followed by these great offices. It is
(he art of selling life assurance by the half-
pint or the half-ounce, and is as completely
distinct from the other branch of the iiulus
try, wherein insurance is sold by the pu u
cheon or the ton, as the purchase of half-H-
-pound of steak is fx’om being a cattle-kin, >
after the fashion of Millionaire Tyson. The
commissions paid are heavy, because it often
costs as much trouble and patience, and aa
much damage to the moral law, to trade ofl
a £lO industrial policy on a servant-girl or a
labourer’s wife, as it does to obtain a £lOOO
proposal under ordinary circumstances. Also
there are other heavy and unusual charges
for whereas the average insurer pays his
premium at the office without any outlay for
collection, the industrial company under-
takes to collect, evex’y week or every month,
perhaps 100,000 or more sepax-ate premiums
of a very few pence or shillings apiece.
Therefore, the latter, like all small retailers,
must demand elxhanced prices. The indivi-
dual who insures to the extent of, say, six-
pence per week, probably receives tlxree-
penn’orth of life-insurance—that is to say,
as much of the commodity as he could get if
he saved up threepence every week for a
year and then insured on the ordinary
annual paymexxt system - and he takes
out the other threepence in wear and
tear of canvasser and collector. Conse-
quently, an industrial company which spends
70 per cent, of its premium income on
management expenses may be just as solvent
as the ordinary insurance company which
expends only 25 per cent. To draw com-
parisons between the two, as sundry New
South Wales legislators attempted to do, is
essentially absurd.
* * #
Industrial insurance would be utterly
extravagant and indefensible but for one
thing—it reaches a class which would seldom
make any provision for the future under any
other system. Its patrons ax-e mostly those
who never save xxp sufficient to pay a yearly
or half-yearly premium in a lump sum, but
who can be induced under certain circum-
stances to do with two drinks less per week
and devote the proceeds to this purpose,
provided the collector reaches them before
these drinks have been consumed. It is a
species of portable enterprise which carries
round a provision for death or old age, and
sells it in small quantities to those who
would never go in search of it, and who
could seldom buy it on wholesale principles.
It is the Indian hawker among insurance
companies, and as such it supplies a want
which the lai’gest and most prosperous insti-
tution could hardly' fill; axxd its x'esults, under
efficient management, are as good as could
reasonably be expected.
# * *
Unfortunately, there is hardly a single
enterprise— not even excepting a land-bank
or a gold-mine in a land where there is no
gold that is less capable of efficient manage-
ment. The very nature of the business is
enormously dangerous, we do not say invari-
ably fatal, to it. The work is necessarily
conducted by means of canvassers, and even
an efficient canvasser, in. a country which is
already worked to death by all kinds of in-
surance and other roving agents, seldom
brings in each week new business yielding
more than 2s. per week in premiums. He
receives very likely £2 per week as salary or
commission, it being necessary to pay him at
least sufficient for maintenaxxce purposes.
Thus in his first week he probably secures an
actual or nominal, or partly actual and
partly nominal revenue of atxout 25., and
costs the office £2. If he continues steadily
at the work for two years, and obtains the
same amount of new business every week,
which, we presunxe, is not always the case,
he is then bringing in a revenue of £lO Bs.
per week, and still costing only £2. The
opportunities for fraud under this system
are of the most ruinous description. One
agent of such a company (not the Citizens’)
within the writer’s knowledge conducted his
business as follows : For every proposal
bringing in a premium of 9d. per week—the
first ten weeks being paid in advance —he
I'eceived £1 as commission. Therefore he
issued policies gratis to all who would accept
them, paid the ten weeks’ premium,
amounting to 7s. 6d., out of his own pocket,
and drew 20s from the office on each pro-
posal. Every policy was allowed to lapse
when the next premium fell due, conse-
quently the company lost 12s. 6d. on each
transaction, and insured a large number of
people for a considerable term without receiv-
ing anything in return. There is apparently
no method by which this description of fraud
can be guarded against; the office relies upon
the honour of the canvasser, and as indus-
trial canvassing often is, like the time-pay-
ment oleograph business, one of the last
refuges of the destitute and uttex-ly seedy,
he is not invariably “square.” An insur-
ance company conducted upon ordiriaxy
principles makes a profit out of policies
which lapse after the payment of one or two
premiums ; to an industrial company these
lapses are a ghastly loss, and when they
become sufficiently numerous they mean
ruin.
# # •
In the Citizens’ Office something like 50
per cent, of the policies would appear o
have lapsed, with tolerably disastrous con
sequences. In 1887, according to the dffiues
before us, the company saved about 48 pel
cent, of its premium income, leaving 5- pe
cent, for losses by deaths and woi k")»
expenses. In 1888, the saving dwindled
36 per cent. ; in 1880, it stood at rather 0
than 28 per cent. ; in 1890, it represent
32 per cent. ; and in 1891, it fell to 0


.}(), The institution is undoubtedly solvent
l-tipon its own published figures at the
date of if B bist balance-sheet, but if this pro-
ceSS continues indefinitely, it will, so far as
u - e can see, as certainly become insolvent
sooner or later. A concern whose balance
of revenue over expenses has fallen from 48
to 26 per cent, is—unless it retrenches —on
the high-road towards a condition of things
where there will be no balance at all,
and oM premiums will go in commissions,
death-claims, and office charges. Even now,
but for the accidental enhancement of its
property values by certain city improve-
ments, its assurance fund would, so far as we
can reckon, be insufficient to cover its con-
tingent liabilities, as shown by the actuary’s
report, even though these are calculated
after excluding the liability upon policies of
three years’ standing, i.e., the liability upon
the enormous increase of business which is
held up as a proof of the society’s success.
The “uncalled capital” of £lBO,OOO looks
like a splendid guarantee of solidity, but un-
called capital often proves an exceedingly
intangible asset at the final emergency.
What, for instance, is the value of the un-
called capital of the recently-collapsed Aus-
tralian banking and other companies ? Such
capital is seldom or never called up unless a
company goes insolvent, and after the in-
solvency hardly anyone pays if he can
possibly avoid doing so.
• # #
We attach no importance whatever, one
way or the other, to the adulatory notices
from the English financial press read in
the Assembly by Mr. Garvan. The
purely financial press of England, judging
from facts within our own knowledge, is the
most utterly corrupt and venal, and at
times ignorant, institution under the sun.
ft booms wild-cat mines in exchange for
money or promoters’ shares ; and whoops on
commission for the securities of bogus South
American States, while it blasts the securities
of Australian Governments which take no
pains to propitiate its hirelings. There are,
of course, some few exceptions to this rule,
but they are so few that The Bulletin
would strongly advise the Citizens’ Company
to bury its commendatory notices deep in the
boundless sea and forget that they ever
existed.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The P.M. at Broken Hill.
Consider the utterly unfair position in
which Mr. Johnston, police-magistrate, has
now been placed at Broken Hill. In that area
of raging dispute, two factions are admittedly
at war. It is not necessary to insinuate that
Mr. Johnston is a partial sympathiser with
either side. Let it be conceded that he is an
unconcerned machine, a police-magistrate whose
sole aim is to hold an absolute equipoise between
the two parties and to give both sides fair play
and unchallengeable justice. Yet we read in the
newspaper accounts of Broken Hill doings that
t he whole of the precautions for the dramatic-
ally military defence of the Proprietary Mine are
under the supervision and direction of—Mr.
Johnston aforesaid. Now, suppose this is
true, or even partially true, and that there
should be a fight between the miners on
strike ami the “ garrison ” defending these
military earthworks and rifle-pits : Will
Mr. Johnston be expected to preside
magisterially over the charge, or charges, laid
against any man, or men, who may be arrested
for having taken a part in such battle, on the
wrong side, or, for that matter, on the right
side, whichever that may be held to be ? Will
Mr. Johnston, as “ general ” directing, or even
partially controlling, directly or indirectly, one
party of combatants, and thus necessarily con-
stituting himself a fighting partisan, be ex-
pected to dispense undefiled and dispassionate
justice ? Is Mr. Johnston godlike that he
perform this miracle ? Mr Johnston,
h.M., is in an altogether false position,
tl -ii * S an instinctive sportsman, and the
brill of any conflict in which a sportsman
personally takes part fires his sympathies,
intensifies his prejudices, and distorts his
judgment. Mr. Johnston, as personifying the
Law, must be on the side that will be
accounted in the right; yet the excitement of
iard knocks and bloody exchanges cannot fail
o act on his instincts as a sportsman and bias
a !\ ls 'Jews of the merits and demerits of the
P. les involved. The goddess of Justice is
md and cannot light ; but Mr. Johnson, who
uay fight, will necessarily be blind only to the
dh °/ I” 8 comrades-in-arms, while the

■ amied from the commencement, and their
u- Ua illegal deeds will be magnified in

“ 8 circumstances. The question born of
Ipmr cons iderations is : Who should be the
pv, l !ul ate leader °f tllis civil and quasi-military
d P fr} tloU? Who should head the theatrical
Wii K Irs1 rs °J well-advertised bastions ?

Ihe Piety Racket.
(H S vv?, 0h of Magistrates at Parramatta
niaVi Vi ‘ llst week discharged witli a caution a
o{ v , lm , 10 Was brought before it accused either
of snnl a ’^ y ° 1 ’ of havin g no lawful visible means
liuvo lP° rt —we don't know which, but it must
ekiiurf 1 ° n >° ot . tlloSe °Hences. His way of
then* 9 jn° i, a l' vc .l'* 100, l was to pray in public,
Ink,, l )la cc a sixpence in his own hat and to
•Hind lor additional contributions. It
is only the scale of this fellow’s operations
which is novel. The principles of his scheme
are by this time familiar enough to the public
of Australia, when applied to a wholesale trans-
action. He is merely tv modest imitator of
numerous great men, some of whom tire at the
present time enjoying considerable celebrity.
IS early all the bogus financial institutions have
been started by their talented promoters on the
same lines. They have prayed in public—the
vestry and the Bible-class and the holiness
meeting and the mission to the heathen have
been the scene of strenuous and ostentatious
devotion on their part. They smelled their
hats in public longer than ordinary worshippers
and seemed reluctant to remove their noses
from the mellifluous odour of the sweat-band.
They recited the responses with the loudest
sort of fervour. They yowled the hymns in
strange keys with a shrillness or a sonorous-
ness which maddened the choirmaster and
deeply impressed the congregation. And when
in due course their phenomenal piety had im-
pressed a wide circle, they put a sum of money
into their financial hat and invited all their
respectful admirers to follow their example.
The only difference between their system and
that of the Parramatta pietist is—the latter
never pretended that the admirers of his religious
demonstrations would ever see their money
again, or derive any advantage from the invest-
ment until they got compound interest in
Heaven ; the bank-boomer pretended that what-
ever the public placed in the hat still belonged to
themselves, and would be employed at usury
for their advantage, and then deliberately
juggled it into his own pocket. The warning
conveyed to the innocent heaven-stormer of
Parramatta may be applied to the larger opera-
tors. The piety racket, as a variation of the
confidence-trick, is just about played out—for
the present.
Police Privilege.
A young married lady, moving in the polite
society of Toorak (Melb.), was recently singled
out for persecution by two despicable police-
men, who made sundry insinuations against the
character of her fashionable residence, and left
her at liberty to assume that they wished to
preserve a golden silence on the matter. Evi-
dently they had called at the wrong house for
that sort of thing, and reckoned without the
hostess. She had the pair chivvied off the
premises, laid her case before the Chief Com-
missioner, and demanded an investigation
which ended in a sudden reduction of the police
force by a pair of rascals. That a mere dis-
missal from their billets should be considered
sufficient punishment for these intending black-
mailers is striking evidence of the general tone
of police morality. Their only defence was the
plea that they had acted on false information
regarding this particular house, or called at the
wrong house by mistake—a defence which, by
the way, is uncommonly rough upon such a
haughty suburb as Toorak. The boldness of
these men suggests that they have probably
called at “ right ” houses and made no mistake
ere now. Maybe more than one frisky dame of
the district has been frightened into “squaring”
these snakes in uniform, who, in that case, may
still live comfortably upon a modest income of
hush-money. But this casual supposition is not
to the main point. The non-prosecution of the
dismissed constables (whose names were kept
secret from the public, out of considera-
tion for their feelings, we presume) is a
partial sanction of their offence, and their
offence is in the very blood of the force.
The custom of “ foxing ” couples in the gloom,
when he ought to be looking out for quite a
different variety of suspicious circumstance in
the interests of public safety, is followed by the
average policeman from generation to genera-
tion. His curiosity as to relations between the
sexes is the chief feature in his lurking,
unlovable character, and when he discovers an
amorous something that is of no moment to the
world at large, he not infrequently tries to turn
his miserable knowledge to account. Hired
for the suppression of dangerous evils he usually
does as little as possible in that way for his
wages. Much watching of al fresco courtship
enervates the gentleman in bine, and prevents
him from fixing his eye on the murderous lar-
rikin even if he hadn’t lost his zeal for work that
involves danger. He needs reforming altogether
does the policeman. He is too fond of winking
at the vice that stalks in the market-place and
of sniffing round the corner for indiscretions in
which he has no official concern—of trying to
get a hold over feeble women when he ought to
be aweing muscular men. And all the while
he is allowed to share with “representative”
persons the privilege of committing glaring
outrages without being sent to prison when he is
found out.
A Curse Come Home to Boost.
That semi-intelligent politician, B. R. Wise,
has been wandering in his mind a good deal
since Sir Henry Parkes’ leading-string slipped
out of his nose-ring. As an expounder of econo-
mics, Mr. Wise owes the small share of noto-
riety which he lias achieved mainly to having
once distinguished himself by informing a body
of Balmain ironworkers that, under Freetrade,
they must hold themselves in readiness to, if
necessary, transfer their labour to some other field
of industry. But just as Dennis, the hangman,
found all his complacent philosophy about the
ease and elegance, the grace and charm, of
execution by the rope, desert him when it came
to his turn to indulge in those delights, Mr.
Wise has got into a most distressed state of
mind since the accuracy of his doctrine has
received an aggravatingly awkward endorse-
ment by the closing of many great tinplate
works in Freetrade (beat Britain, owing to the
operation of the MTviNUEY protective tariff in
the United States. Protectionists having in-
dulged in a mild chuckle over this striking
example of an ill argument coming home to
roost in the coop of its breeder, Mr. Wise
immediately raised a voice of lamentation and
weeping, and, amidst appreciative sobs from an
audience worthy of him, said, in a heart-broken
way, that he didn’t envy the feelings of the
persons who could rejoice over an event which,
by depriving people of employment, occasioned
milch undeserved suffering to men, women and
children. This lopsided way of regarding
things is Mr. Wise’s patent property, and
trespassers will bo prosecuted. The trouble
between him and the Protectionists is just this
—Wise alleges in a general way that, under
Preetrade, people may have to transfer their
industry to some employment different from
that which they are skilled in, and the Protec-
tionists cheerfully agree with him and heave
his own argument at him every time, and give
a sort of horse laugh when a particularly glar-
ing example of its correctness looms large.
When this happens Mr. Wise goes round and
asks all the people he meets to listen to that
diabolical and fiendish laughter, and tries to
get them to believe that it is not his grotesque
fix that has produced the merriment, but the
sufferings of poor women and children. Poor
Mr. Wise’s disaster is accentuated by the fact
that the misfortune which Preetrade has in-
flicted upon the unlucky workers in England
stands contrasted with the more than corres-
ponding benefit which Protection has conferred
upon the same class of individuals in the United
States. While several large tin-working estab-
lishments have been closed in the Preetrade
country, 34 similar factories are being erected
in the Protectionist one. The obvious inference
is, of course, calamitous for the “ transfer their
labour to other fields of industry ” young man.
Clearly the only relief for the English tin-plate
hand is for him to transfer his labour from
England to the United States—to escape, in
fact, from the starvation which “ the glorious
principles of economic freedom ” impose on
him in order to participate in the ample pro-
vision which “restriction ” offers for his accept-
ance. We shall contemplate with attentive
amusement Mr. Wise’s wrigglings to crawl out
of Ids exceedingly embarrassing fix. “ Eat—or
be eaten ! ”
Cat-Tail and Cow-Tail.
Condensed commentary on civilisation from
West Australian paper :
Ten blacks were tried at the Police-court here to-day
(August 6) for breaking out of Wyndham gaol and stealing
tucker and breaking the telegraph-line. Seven of them
were sentenced to twelve lashes, and the other three were
sent back to Wyndham to complete their original
sentence. The cat-o’-nine-tails supplied is useless. The
Government might as well have sent a cow-tail. Water
very scarce.
The cat supplied by the Government is useless!
M hat au amount of desolation is conveyed in
that sentence ! The West Australian beak
yearns over that good old cat. He loves every
knot on its gnarled surface, and he stands by
and watches affectionately while it cuts great
streaks on the hide of his brother man ; also,
he strains his capacious ear to catch the full
music of the howl which it extracts when it
comes down like a pickaxe on the hinder part
of the victim. There is always a decent
gathering of magistrates at a West Australian
flogging, and the bleary eyes of the respectable,
old, lag-descended fathers light up over it, and
their palsied white heads wag approvingly, and
their pendulous countenances grow more cheer-
ful, and the rolls of unwholesome fat behind their
necks look a shade less unwholesome than usual
as the ceremony proceeds. But at the date of the
above-quoted paragraph the good old men were
feeling injured. Seven niggers had just been
flogged, and they didn’t shriek over it with
sufficient energy to make a West Australian
holiday, and their backs had more skin left on
them when the proceedings were over than the
audience thought proper under the circum-
stances. The Government cat may look a suffi-
ciently horrible weapon with its numerous tails
and the big, hard knots on each; but to an
official who has been accustomed to see petty
aboriginal larcenists flogged by gigantic bullock-
drivers with saplings, it lacks vigour, and
trenches on the commonplace. They evidently
feel much as the untamed Indian might do if
reduced to keeping a barber’s shop in his old
age; after the bloodthirsty joy of tearing off
the human scalp the mere pleasure of occasion-
ally snipping a piece off the ear is stale, flat,
and exceedingly monotonous.
Lady Kintore and a number of well-meaning
Adelaide idiots have started a “ Rent-Aid
Association,” whose special business is to search
out poor widows and miserable, deserted
wives, &c., &c., plant them in homes rent-free
—to be called the Kintore Homes—or pay, in
full or in part, their rents for them. The
Bulletin would much rather be inclined to
commend a tenants’ No-Rent League, bred in
and nurtured on anti-capitalistic rebellion, than
this misapplied offshoot of bogus benevolence.
The real effect of such gammon will be to
subsidise the rent-ogres all round. The
subscriptions will merely find their way
into the blood-leeching pouches of the land-
lords and unearned - increment men. The
well-fed property-owners will be able to
raise rents all round, as their present most
uncertain tenants will become their surest
payers. No .wonder the close-lipped, sleek
obesities who live on usurious rents accord the
scheme their oily approval and proclaim them-
selves the special friends of lorn widowhood and
betrayed wifedom. The salvation of the poor
lies in the opposite direction—the depreciation
of land-values in their appurtenance to private
owners, the pulling-down of rent-rates, and the
ultimate abolition of all rents except those in
the nature of occupation-fees under perpetual
leases. The Adelaide Rent-Aid Association
will give aid to a hundred widows so that it and
its congenital brood of non-contributing land-
lords may screw ten times as much out of ten
thousand other tenants.

A Charters lowers (N.Q.) correspondent wires:
A b!g public meeting was held at the Miners’
Hall, Charters lowers, on Sunday night, to
protest against 1604 names having been struck
oft the roll. Ihe hall was packed to overfiow-
mg. Indignant speeches were made, enthusi-
astic resentment was expressed, and resolutions
were carried as follow :
That the many measures passed by the present Govern-
menti m defiance of the people’s wishes, and contrary to
members election - pledges, are not binding on ‘the
country, and should be repudiated by a National Conven-
tion representing all citizens.
That the A.L.F. be requested to take steps to initiate
the movement at once.
That such Convention sit at Rockhampton, and that it
consist of delegates from every constituency, said dele-
gates to be elected on the basis of manhood suffrage.
-This Charters Towers outburst is only a solitary
explosion among general Queensland pyrotech-
nics that indicate that the Griffilwraith Go-
vernment stinks in the nostrils of the people
North,South,Central and West. If theChartcrs
lowers and Kennedy district workers generally
only abstain from trivial internal dissensions
and unite solidly at the coining general election,
they can sweep the polls and '‘return Labour-
members for all the seats in their areas. The
damnable arrogance of the capitalistic roll-
falsifiers may be imagined from the fact.that a
large number of the 1901 electors whose names
had been rubbed off the voting-lists were
actually present at the meeting of protest. The
Slavery Government will stop at no infamy of
oppression in the endeavour to cheat their fore
seen doom.
A Foreign-trade paper in Sydney gives a
fresh example of the affliction of mental squint
which disfigures advocates of that half witted
creed. Because certain storekeepers—who mav
or may not be Protectionists—in Grafton and
Glen limes are agitating for direct over-sea
trade between European ports and the Clarence
River, and a Grafton Protectionist paper sup-
poits their views, the little Sydney Foreign-
trade journal begins to rumple the straws in its
hair and to slaver long-primer. “It is a curious
coincidence, it raves, “ that the newspapers
which advocate a restrictionist policy' are the
\ eiy that cry out for a foreign shipping
trade.” These wanderings are due to several
aberrations on the part of the patient. One is
that protective duties must decrease the bulk
of foreign exchange, whereas they can only
alter the character of the importations. The
other is that because a storekeeper on the Clar-
ence desires to see direct shipments to that
river from abroad, he can’t be a consistent Pro-
\\ hat the Clarence people really
want is just what the Brisbane people, the
Rockhampton people, and the Townsville
people required—and secured. That is their
own share of the import shipping trade. The
merchants at northern ports get sick of payin< r
a commission tax to the big houses of Sydney*
and as soon as the local consumption is sufficient
to use up an assorted cargo, they want that
cargo to be sailed into their own port. This is
reasonable and natural, and has no relation,
however remote, to the fiscal dispute. Our
distracted contemporary had best smooth down
its rumpled straws, and compose its distraught
brain till next full moon.
Editor Bulletin, —Following up your recent
leader on the proposed Queensland exodus to
Argentine, it may be asked, seeing that one of
the financial impatiences of the Griffilwraith
policy is the selling wholesale of large areas of
ktate land, what is to prevent the sanguine
leaders of the “New Australia” movement
from acquiring their projected Canaan in
Queensland, building up their communistic re-
public right here on the spot, and striving to
communicate the contagion of their example to
others ? If they have cash and hearts sufficient
to found a productive commonwealth “on the
dreary plains of La Plata,” surely they have
enough, and to spare, of the sinews of success to
establish the living actuality of their ideal in
our own country ? There is only false heroism
in the migration, anyway. Co-operation, pro-
perly directed, can be just as effectively emanci-
pating in Queensland as in Argentina. —H.C.

Argus reasoning on the Beulah (Vic.) poisoning
case is decidedly shaky :
The motive was sordid and base-just such a motive as
might be looked tor having regard to the relationship
between murderess and victim. Cornwall and O’Brifx
were living in adultery, and the woman, having -Town
tired of her companion, attempted to relieve herself of
him and to secure his property for herself and another
paramour by the one stroke.
To the Argus, then, it appears that because a
woman isn’t married to a man she is the more
likely to murder him, in order that she may
share his money with another man whom she
has no intention of marrying. This is gospel
according to Mrs. Grundy. As a matter of
fact, the motive for cold-blooded murder is not
so strong with wideawake concubines as with
wives of limited understanding, who occasion-
ally poison one husband because they wish to
possess a second. Seeing that O’Brien merely
“ lived with” Cornwall, she would naturally
he free from that awful regard for “ propriety ”
which has caused so many wives to commit a
murder in preference lo making a clean holt of
if, whilst the inference that paramours arc over
ready lo kill a man for i he sake of Ids property
is a respectable piece of nonsense.

SJC North, Sydney, Sept. 14,1892.
My Dear Mooradikda,—
The ’92A.J.C. meeting- will always stand out as com-
mencing with one of the most lively and well-groomed
Derby Day crowds that Ilandwiok has ever seen. The
weather was pretty, and so were the frocks, taking the
average—because, of course, you couldn’t avoid seeing
the inevitable punting rainbow-woman who clothes her-
self as nearly like her favourite horse’s jockey as circum-
stances permit.
The Governor’s box was well filled, principally by'ice-
regal aliens and strangers, for Lord Jersey wasn’t there: he
was represented by a daughterlet in fawn shades, Chum-
ley and Goschen. Lady Hopetoun cut the most attractive
figure in the V.R. group. She sported a fawn corduroy
plain skirt and basque bodice, made with black silk and
white satin confused sort of front, ending in what ought
to be called Masonic tabs of white satin hand-painted in
pink roses: black feathered hat, black parasol and .cor-
sage-cluster of pink roses. In the absence of Lord Jersey,
Lady Hopetoun fastened the Darby band Camoola’s
neck—which proves that her ladyship is progressing in
the art and science of how to become a successful
Governor’s wife. Last year, nothing could induce her to
decorate the Derby winner.
Lady Norman wore a very handsome but unseasonable
get-up of heavy black velvet, with sleeves of stamped,
velvet and trimmings of black ostrich-feathers and black
jet, with a touch of crushed strawberry; bonnet trimmed
with black lace and a corsage bouquet of white flowers.
Miss Norman was quite dowdy, which was a pity, for she
is rather a pretty girl. Her frock was a crushed-looking
mauve ottoman silk limply trimmed with gimp and mauve
chiffon ; black straw hat trimmed with bows.
On the lawn, tracking down the pretty costumes was
just like catching butterflies—everybody looked so gay.
Mrs. Burdekin’s struck me as the best turn-out. Her
notion was a wallflower-red-and-lavender shot-silk, made
with a trained umbrella-skirt, with a narrow hem of wall*
flower-red velvet bordered with beads, Figaro jacket of
wallflower-red, edged with beads, tiny bonnet and sun-
shade of the shot-silk. Mrs. Willie Mitchell ran a good
second in fawn crepon (fawn was the leading colour, and
crSpon the leading stuff on Derby Day) of very smart cut
t rimmed with pink roses. Another effective fawn crepon
was worn by Miss Nellie Hill, and trimmed with bands of
dark-brown silk, and little brown rosettes and epaulettes
of Maltese lace ; Leghorn hat fixed up with brown ribbons
and pink roses.
Miss Maude Gowlland’s gown was sui generis—& black,
silky figured stuff, with big gigot sleeves of black velvet
trimmed with hoops of emerald-green velvet. Mrs.
(Mayoress) Manning wore a very nobby array of steel-grey
poplin, mingled with black velvet and jet; her daughter
was in grey crepon of very fashionable structure, and
trimmed with flesh-pink silk. Myra Kemble wore a very
sporting coat of black cloth, trimmed with lines of gold
tinsel braid and tortoise buttons.
I was never more flabbergasted in my life than at the
aspect of the d.c. at Ryley’s farewell “ Gondoliers’ ”
matinee, the other Wednesday. Instead of rows of dis-
consolate maidens the house was filled with determined-
looking matrons! There were a few girls in the stalls,
but all over the house the bonnets were ten to every one
hat. But perhaps it was well the girls didn’t go, because
the hero tripped (for I believe the first time in Australia)
in one of his whirling dances and nearly knocked .1 fat and
breathless male chorister through an o.p. “wa'l.”
At the first Musin concert in the Centennial Hall on
Bth inst., there was a good attendance of musical people,
but the only gown was worn on the platform by Madame
Musin—a pink silk, scooped out like a sugar-trowel.
Madame is tall and retrousse, with black hair, and
astonishes you by not wearing a train on the stage. As a
general thing, the higher the soprano the longer the tail.
M. Ovide Musin (who went to school, it seems, with
Monsieur Wiegand) is the very image of his billed picture.
I got on my perch at Her Majesty’s, A la sheep-dog—
that is, almost running over the skulls of my fellow-man,
for most of the floor-space was packed with Johnnies long
ere the curtain rose. The seven and-sixpenny reserved
people were the very top-skim of local society (for one
always counts Govmentouse out on such occasions), and if
you missed any home-grown title in the full sweep of the
brilliant circle (Miss Manning looked fetching in pale-blue
covered with tulle and diamond ornaments, and Miss
Hill’s bright dragon-blue silk added a spot of colour to an
otherwise luke-warm row) it was simply because the said
title couldn’t possibly get a seat. Which reminds me
that at the more popular premieres—like this Gaiety one

The fun and frolic on mail-steamers must sometimes
verge on the Hilarious, if one can he)ie\ e all the
stories one hears. For instance, I am told that on a recent
voyage a certain section of the first salooners had a very
scriramagy time one moonlit night. A vivacious hut
portly matron was sleeping on deck when one or two mad
wags conceived the idea of softly tying a rope to a leg of
the lounge and hauling the whole thing along the deck.
But owing to the ’dupois of the lady the rope snapped,
and then a hold spirit actually tied the cord round the
matron’s ankle and hauled her out of the chair. She
awoke then, of course—hut the story doesn’t end with
anybody dangling from the yardarm.
Talking about ships. There is nothing new under the
sun, certainly, but surely that is no reason why people
should go on deliberately perpetrating old sea jokes with
dreadful monotony. Passengers per mail-boat wiP, 1 am
sure, feel grateful to me for pointing out to young Moods,
en route, that to pin “ not wanted on the voyage ” labels
on to the backs of skittish lady passengers is i ow rococo.
It was considered screamingly funny for the first few
hundred times, but now the youth who is seen sneaking
about the deck chair of the ship-flirt with a gummy white
label, runs a great risk of being dropped gehtly down the
coalhole.

There was a vast audience at Her Majesty’s
(,Sydney) on Saturday night when the Gaiety com-
pany started its,garish career with. “Faust up to
Date.” It was, not a specially discriminating
crowd,at the early stages,pf the show, for, during
t,he first act, it manifested a. reckless tendency to
cheer for everybody and everything; but later on
it gathered itself together apd settled down to
screech to the bitter end for Lonpep and Alice
Leamar. It screeched in a. perfunctory way for
some of the others, but for these two it yelled all
it w.as able to. “Faust ” itself is the smallest part
of the show. It has a Hn-de-siecle mephistopheles
in it, with a small “m ” and a red plug hat,
which D eminently suited for a generation that
believe in a devil as its devil-haunted
fathers used to do. Heis the only reasonably definite
individual in the cast, and even lie isn’t definite
enough to take an, affidavit upon ; the balance is
loose arid unattached gorgeonsiiess and “'karara-
boom-de-py.” “Faust” is a burst, of red-painted
and ai;t-decorated frenzy ; ; a mad panorama with
its hair on fire and fireworks in, its tail, It. leaves
behind, ft a dizzy vision, concerning an endless
processipn of, opium-eaters, nioiiarehs, barmaids,
dudes, legs, professional beauties, prophets, uiono-
rpaniacs, dervishes, gods, hogs, gas-lights, soldiers,
drunkards, deranged wattle, chameleons, gladiar
tors, and, the big, r,ed bottles escaped, out of a,
thousand chemists’ windows. It suggests a fight,
between an, art gallery and a wholesale jewelJery
emporium ap much as.anything else, and it doesn’t
even suggest that very, distinctly. The grey-
bearded lichens of old ago have grown in the
chinks of its senile jokes, and the dialogue is
feeble, and the songs arc pot too brilliant, and
almost the only scrap of music which lives in
ope’s recollection after lie has fallen dizzily down

the stair and pot away, is the little duet between
Martha and the evening-dress devil in the top-
hat. Also, the show, which travels furiously in
the first,act, drags a good deal in tho. second, and
the famous “coster song ” is a weariness in the
operatic flesh.

Alice Lcamar’s dancing is the feature
which brings down the house more than all the
rest of the exhibition combined.. T.hat little, lady,
dances as nd other person of our acquaintance,
ever danced before,’ and her style is partly that of,
a spider" getting’ enthusiastically off a hot stove
and partly that of tlie native kangaroo with
golden hair trying to break a record over a series
of fences, and singing as lie gobs. If she doesn’t
hit her eye with her feet some night, of get her
boots entangled in her back-hair, all will'be well.
Lonnen, who is the other leading feature, is a
pale and clean-shaved devil in dress clothes as
hereinbefore mentioned, with a great fund of
facial expression and a flow of soul. He is humor-
ous all the time, but be is most humorous when he
is buried in thought, and expressing his feelings
only with his eye and his legs, and when he
is not singing coster ditto*. Courtneidge is an
individual whose fun consists largely,of a rpd nose,
and,much ancient business and beer-froth, and
the present writer waits tp see him in a part where
the nose is of a more sober hue before pronouncing
judgment. Marion Hood is larger than she used
to be, and much of the glamour has gone out of
her; and the rest of tlie company is lost, ip the
general mdgnificence of tlie shivoo, and consists
mostly of liair and legs. Even Faust, perpetrated
in an 'agreeable fashion by Addie Conyers, is
hardly distinguishable in the rush. The amount
of hair in this performance runs into bales, and
tlie legs are laid on in tons, and at least 8 cwt. of
paint has been used in the scenery, with, results
that are too gaudy for adequate description. Also
the “book.of the opera” is a thing to be avoided.
It hasn’t got Alice Ijeamar’s frantic dance in it,,
nor Lonnen’s leg's and his expressive countenance,
nor the skirts, nor the hair, nor anything else but
the dry bones of the performance, and these, when
viewed in print, are very dry, and bony beyond
expression.

“ A l , Village? Priest ” (taken from the:
French) is founded on the. iniquity of,a famous
judge. 11 is sins are visited upon everybody con-
cerned in the play, and as a source of all round,
woe there is nothing to touch him on the modern,
stage. An escaped convict, , who. rushes on at.
intervals and conceals himself,in-a long ulster,.
has for nineteen years been expiating the murder
committed by this dead ornament of the Bench.. The
corpse, thus i mproperly avenged, was the father on
tin; ingenue who subsequently gives her heart to the
judge’s son, and her mother is,repenting, in a half-
and-half, fashionable manner, the fact that she
formerly carried on with the.parent of her would-
be son-in-law. The good priest,, having, received!.
thi> death-bed confession of the judicial slaughterer,
and also having granted absolution to his paramour, ,
is weighed down with the, awful knowledge"
that things are getting very mixed. - A doubt as •
to whether the judge’s son hasn’t really contem-
plated marriage with his own half-sister is not
cleared up, however, and the, pfiest’s approval ot .
this intended union, in the. first, instance, 18 a ‘
decidedly weak spot in a complicated narrative.

ought to break the sea! of the, coni’ ssional aim 1
clear the name of the hunted “ lag” in the ulstci,
but he probably wouldn't see any necessity f (l1 ’ s( | ‘
doing if the judge's son hadn’t already discoveiec
that Ins wicked papa wilfully .sent the wrong man 1
to penal seivitud.e. The priest is a model o

oath. At last he appeals to Heaven for a mirac c
to teach him his duty, and in the pfde glare o
stage moonlight a leaf of his Bible is turner
over by the invisible hand of Providence o
by a string, and then the priest feels impelled -

At Melbourne Athenaeum, last Week, Mrs.
Dr. Brainerd Ryder, a scholastic looking Yankee
lady, with no idea of dramatic effect, delivered a
series of three lectures on Hindoo, Parsee, and
Mahoromedan life in India, as she presumably saw
it through her spectacles. The entertainment
invites direct comparison with that of the Von
Finkelstein, and suffers severely in consequence.
The massive charmer from Jerusalem was a glib
talker, who persuaded the audience that she was
painting 1 her impressive word pictures from
nature, but Mrs. Ryder is a cold-blooded doctor,
addicted to referring to notes, and connecting her
remarks with “er-r-r.” The matter of the lec-
tures, though always more or less interesting,
seems to have been collected from books.
Her perorations are particularly suggestive
of something one has read, and her way
of walking off when it’s all over leaves you to
feel surprised at finding that she doesn’t come
back and say something -else. In short, Mrs.
Rvder is lacking in several essential characteristics
of the successful show-woman. Yon Finkelstein
1.193(3 but a few supernumeraries to illustrate her
yarns, and displayed them to good purpose. In
this case the platform is loaded up with unin-
telligent people, disguised as natives of the East,
who illustrate nothing so forcibly as the lecturess’s
ignorance of stage management. Hence Mrs,
Doctor Ryder falls flat. She should hire another
lady to till her place, and then perhaps the des-
cription of how the sacred vultures swarm to the
Tower of Silence and pick every vestige of a
Parsee corpse a way from its skeleton (in the space
of from 30 to 33 minutes by the Waterbury) might
provoke a passing shudder,

“Silver King” has fetch d them every
night at the Princess’, and there seems to be no
financial reason why “Hamlet” should take its
place on Saturday. Walter Bentley is understood
to have come to the end of his engagement, how-
ever, and the management couldn’t let him depart
without appearing in the usual round of popular
impersonations. Managements were ever thought-
ful in this respect. Therefore, Walter Bentley
was induced to prolong his engagement for six
nights, in order that an anxious populace might
get passing glimpses of him in “Hamlet,” “The
Bells,” and “David Garrick.” The last-named
will be played for his farewell benefit on Friday,
23rd.

The Prahran (Melb.) Amateur Operatic
Society have this year given a more than credit-
able performance of “ Les Cloches.” In respect
of chorus-singing the show soared rather above,
than below, ordinary professional form, and the
principals were mostly guilty of a huge vocal
success. Also, the acting of Germaine, Serpo-
G the Bailie, Gobbo, and more beside, indicated
that Victorian native talent in the comic opera
me is worth more managerial attention than it
seems to get. Mr. James Ure, as musical-
