At Poverty Point.
The Gaiety Co., 80 strong-, arrived in Sydney by steamer
from Brisbane on Friday afternoon, and the Comic Opera
Co., 75 strong, sailed for Maoriland at midnight of the
same day. Their season was the biggest ever Known in
Sydney.
The chief “ mute ” at the recent big Chinese funeral at
Sydney was a well-known tragedian whom the new school
of acting has pushed from his stool.
An at one time well-known and prosperous Australian
circus-master went last week to the “Home for the
Decayed ” at Liverpool, N.S.W.
Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis has succeeded fairly as Mrs.
Oatley, in the “ Jealous Wife,” in London. By the way,
the jealousy used to he all on the other side.
The severest thing the Melbourne evening papers have
to say of any play : “ This drama is not devoid of literary
merit.
Helen Hart (leothress) modestly explains that wit and
humour is a gift which she is linable to hide even at a
Cremation meeting,
“ Confusion ” is advertised as “ Derrick’s masterpiece/’
for the reason that nobody ever heard of Derrick in con-
nection with any other piece.
One of the most ingeniously nasty show-notices we
have seen for some time is Sydney Evening News’ critique
on Musin—a friend of Wiegand, by the way.
When Miln was playing Shakespeare, in Melbourne, he
once gave gratis the whole dress-circle of the Opera
House for a theatre-party given by the proprietors of a
local paper. All the guests, excepta few “in the know,”
worried theirbrains by abstruse calculations as tohovvmuch
the great and gorgeous picnic cost their hosts, while
Miln got the credit of having attracted an intelligent and
well-dressed crowd by the intrinsic merit of his acting.
“ Violin taught by a lady.”—Advt. Next pleasantest
thing to their shaving you.
John Watson, M.P. (Q.), advocates the imposition of a
tax of 3d. per head upon all persons who attend theatres.
That would kill off the deadheads, anyhow'.
Max O’Rell has given a sort of a benefit to Brisbane
hospital. No man know's when he may be crippled by-
falling over one of his own jokes.
Though the “Fringe of Society” has been one of very
few paying successes this season, says The Bulletin’s
London correspondent, I do not vary my opinion that it
is a “ rotter,” as Fred. Leslie would say. It has been a
success, but a succis de curiosite. All the world went to
see Mrs. Langtry’s gowns, and her marvellous suite of
diamond and turquoise ornaments —the gift, it is said, of
Lord Lurgan. They are certainly worth looking at, and
are the envy of all womankind,
London Gaiety Co. fixed the prices for their proposed
night in Newcastle (N.S.W.) rather too high, and the
booking results were so meagre that the management
cried off. Newcastle likes its pleasure cheap. The
local Musical Union announces the performance of “La
Fille de Madame Angot ” next w'eek.
Kate Bishop (Mrs. Lohr) is teaching elocution in
Sydney.
Said that Emily Soidene is likely to re-open the now
closed Sydney Opera House with a yet-to-be-gathered
opera company.
Harry Rickards’ only son “Syd.” died on Friday (9th)
and was buried at Waverley the following day. This must
have been a great blow to his doting parents.
Clara Thompson (Mrs. Bracy), sister to the evergreen
Lydia, takes Klsie Cameron’s place (on the conclusion of
the latter’r engagement) with the Comic Opera Co. during
the Maoriland tour.
Charles Holloway has organised a Co.—including Alice
Deorwyn (Mrs. Holloway), Mr. and Mrs. (Constance
Deorwyn) Richard Stewart, Oily Deering, and others—for
a Maoriland tour. They take Harvey’s plays—the most
melodramatic stuff known—and some of Pettitt’s, along.
St. Clair, agent.

“•Carrots.”

fitted to go land-booming.

Bathurst (N.S.W.) has three musicians all of the same
name and each a good man—W. H. Naylor, C. V Naylor,
and C. W. Naylor. And how the local press mixes up the
trio !
Queen Victoria visited Buffalo William’s circus the
other day, and was so gratified by the yelling and the
painted Indians and the attack on the Deadwood coach,
that she gave a gold seal to Hon. Bill Cody, and a valu-
able diamond scarf-pin to the cowboy with the longest
hair and the most terrific whoop. Victoria’s taste in
theatrical matters is pretty low-down. If Buffalo Bill had
been a fatal accident in a coal-mine instead of a vociferous
circus-clown he would only have got a collect-telegram
expressing sympathy'.
Stubbs gives a fair original play at Melbourne Alexandra,
but where is the local colour?
Mrs, Walter Bentley is not coming out to Australia,
after all.
An Australian lady is now, as the result of false pre-
tences, nursing carefully a piece of ragged, old tweed,
She was a great admirer of Jennie Lee’s “ Jo,” one of the
finest pieces of character acting ever witnessed, and,
paying the actress a farewell visit, begged as a souvenir
a fragment of his ragged garments. The actress’s boxes
were all packed and corded, but, anxious (o oblige, she
ran downstairs. On her way she met the housemaid.
Ha 1 an idea ; the floorcloth was the very colour, and it
didn’t take long to tear away and dry a scrap.

also a member—Capt. Ginger in rehearsal.

with the “Sunny South” next year, commencing with a
week at the Grand, Islington.

George Rignold intends putting up at Sydney Her

Harry Rickards announces that the rumour as to his
intention of travelling once more arose from his having
lately sold off some superfluous furniture. He has now
engaged a crowd of new European artists, including
Misses Marie Loftus, Georgie Wright, Messrs. Brown,
Newlands, and Leclerq, and proposes to boom the show
business on a larger scale than ever.
“ The Lost Paradise,” in rehearsal at Melbourne Prin-
cess’, is a comedy-drama of great American vogue, about
to be acted in London. Williamson expects it to hook on.

lately, it was the pleasing duty of a lady member of his
Co. to use the curling-tongs on his amaranthine looks.

of a flight of stairs lately, got too near the edge and fell
all the way down. Kicked herself downstairs, in fact.

Apropos the cable re Frances Saville’s brilliant success
at Brussels, Madame Fanny Simonsen writes us that her
daughter did not appear with the Froliques in Sydney,
for she was then still at school at St. Kilda. She appeared
in Sydney a year and a-half later in English opera, and
played Lslia, in “Satanella,” Wanda, in “Grand
Duchess,” &c. She appeared later in opera bouffe With
Majeroni and Wilson—never with the Froliques.

When Alfred Clint was helping W. j. Wilson to paint
scenery for the old Victoria Theatre, Sydney, Clint one
day said : “ Wilson, I wish you would let me away to-
morrow afternoon if you cap. I’ve a little bit of an
engagement.” “ Well,” replied Wilson,you know how
we’re rushed; but is it very important?” “No, not
very,” said Clint, I had arranged to get married
to-morrow, that was all.” Later on, Habbe,
the scenic artist, who in those days somehow never
did cotton to Clint, paid Wilson a visit and engaged
with him in conversation, but took no notice whatever oit
little Clint, who was also present. Noticing this, Wilson
after a time said ; “ Habbe, you know Mr. Clint, surely? ”
“What did I hear you say ?” exclaimed Chut —“ Mr.
Habbe'! Mr. Habbe ! The great artist, Mr. Habhe ! and
here am t in his presence all the time with niy hat on !
Let me apologise—let me grovel.” And off went Clint’s
hat and down he went On his marrow-bones in the
presence of the discomfited Habbe.
A ghastly showman story from Bendigo: A certain
financier rented a shop to two men who Wanted to exhibit
a monster shark. But the weather Was hot, and the
deceased fish went bad the second day, and, finding that
the perfume destroyed business, the pair decamped, and
when the financier went round for his rent he found
nothing but an abandoned shop and a decaying ITcwt. of
horror prone upon the floors Then he hired three men to
bury the accursed object, but being afterwards informed
by a friend that shark’s teeth were valuable, he dug it
up again in the hope of recovering his rent —only to find
that the ruffianly piscatorial syndicate had extracted
every tooth from the festering mass before they Went;
whereupon he had to pay for a second funeral, and was
fined for creating a nuisance, arid all his other tenants
have left on account of the odour which hangs about the
shop and refuses to be shifted.
A Garrick (Sydney) gallery boy, giving gratis a little
thing of his own, between acts, the other evening, when
asked to “ blige again,” replied; “D’yer think I’m goin’
to spile my voice for such a set Of larrikins as you are !”
Mackintosh, a popular London actor, is a native of
Fitzroy, Melb., but never acted in Australia*
Nellie Veitch teaches elocution at twenty Melbourne
schools, and has taught Aim6e Moore. Miss Veitch is a
two-faced woman (all woman are two-faced)—she can
actually transform her features into two distinct per-
sonages, according as Thalia or Melpomene is needed,
and her photographs pass for entirely different indivi-
duals. She acted with Genevieve Ward in Maoriland.
The Royal Pantechnicon of Science and Art was the
title under which the London Alhambra first opened, and
there was prayer by a clergyman !—but now it’s devoted
to nothing but legs.
A London writer ironically says that it is evidently pos-
sible for an actress to live in a manner that is above sus-
picion and yet win a blue-blooded husband.
Little Martin Simonsen, recently from ’Frisco with a
fresh “star” co., being asked by other Yankee entre-
preneurs whether he would advise them to try their
luck in Australia, said: “No, ray frenis, you vill not do
goot; you may draw a full house for von consecutive
night, but certainly for not longair.”
Lynch Family of Bellringers celebrated its “ silver ”
anniversary of “showdom” at Castlemaine, Vic., on 23rd
August last, their first appearance having been in Geelong
(Vic.) on the date named in’67. With the exception of
Henry Lynch, senior, the five members of the first
“family” were all natives of Australia, or, to be par-
ticular, of Geelong ; the other four are still “ ringing,”
but the old man has retired on his laurels. Harry Lynch,
jun., now manages the “family,” which is shortly
travelling into N.S. Wales and Sydney-wards. The
Lynches have many times traversed Australasia, and have
had gay successes in India, China, Japan, Batavia, and
the East generally.
They now call it “ begeflt,”
Harvey Adams, brother of Francis Adams,
died at Brisbane, on Tuesday morning, of con-
sumption, against which he had long and
courageously battled.

Mrs. Beetou says: “Claret should be
sound, fruity, and soft as satin, and all the com-
ponents happily blended into a homogeneous
whole. Easy of digestion, it is a mildly stimu-
lating drink, in every way fulfilling the Scriptural
character as a ‘ wine that maketh glad the heart
of man.’” Innes’s Boar’s Head Clarets, at all
grocers, at Is., Is. 6d., 25., and 3s. a bottle, can
be recommended as purer and bettor wines than
French clarets, and they are only one-third of the
price.

EDMUND FISNER.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The 6pinioti of Peter Newtoil, Abe Hicken,
Nathan, and other supposed pugilistic critics of Mel-
bourne, published before the event, was that Sullivan
couldn’t possibly lose the fight with Corbett. The opinion
of ignorant, un-“ talented ” people was that any huge
beast who hadn’t half-drunk himself to death should be
able to knock out Johnel, and the general public knew
most about it. They always do. An intelligent shearer,
miner, town-bred clerk, wharf-labourer, or even a C.M.G.,
can generally judge, the merits of, say, oarsmen at prac-
tice a lot better than the cognoscenti, who have a horn
genius for picking the Wrong one. Ergo, the typical!
“ sport ” is badly off for brains.
Many of the Melbourne bookmakers talk of not
registering themselves again for courses where the total-
isator is likely to be at work this coming season. This is
very sad. Unfortunately, the bookmaker out of employ-
ment is tit for nothing except to stand at street-corners
and blaspheme.

There passed last year through official Queens-
land “totes” sums amounting to £565,000. The MTi-
wraith tax of 2£ per cent, on that would reach £14,225.

Totalisator “ divvies ” were all big in Adelaide
on Sep. 1. By putting £1 on every nag an investor could
have panned out a profit of £l4 on the day.

At a late Yass (N.S.W.) pigeon-match a good
shot, hut a had scholar, wished the secretary to enter him
“under a consumed name.” The secretary, equal to the
occasion, accordingly put him down as “ Burnt Jack. 71 It
was, he said, the only name he could think of under the
circumstances,

Melbourne Cup day is shown in the calendar as
that of “ All Saints.” There are two saints entered ; St.
Albans II and St. Blaise.
# * #
If Ferris plays with the next Australian Eleven
in England the patriotic sentiment will soon fade right
out of international cricket. Young Woods, the Cam-
bridge man, would seem more acceptable in the team
than Perris, inasmuch as he can pretend to be an Aus-
tralian abroad—though all his cricket has been learnt in
England. Ferris has fairly gone over to the enemy and
turned English while his play was still open to change its
nationality, so to speak, Murdoch and Spofforth will
always be Australian enough for any representative
eleven, but then they are too old, or rather too played-
out, to be desirable acquisitions.
* ‘ * #
Those keen Bristol people who went to such
expense to secure Ferris for Gloucestershire must be
sadly disappointed with their speculation, says an
English correspondent. The once famous county of the
Graces is almost at the bottom of the list this year, only
Sussex, which is out of all form, being below them.
Ferris is an expensive frost. He cost the rent of a house
whilst he was qualifying, and he had to he provided with
a billet, to keep him going when there is no cricket to
make expenses out of. I don’t think any other county
will be inclined to try the same experiment.
# # *
The cable reports of wonderful performances
by Woods or Ferris are growing monotonous. Neither of
the Australian-Anglo bowlers has a remarkable average
for the county season.
# * *
Spofforth is quite out of it now, He won’t even
play for the M.O.C.

* # #
Sydney Evening News : “ The circumstance of
a man having passed what is commonly regarded as the
prime of pugilistic life does not, it is true, always tell
conclusively against his chance of defeating an opponent
who happened to be younger, and who, in all other
respects, may be a pretty equal match for him. There
was, for example, the memorable case of Tom Sayers, who
was about thirty-five when he encountered Heenan, who,
besides being his junior by many years, was a giant in
comparison with the indomitable English champion.”
The Evening News, we hope, doesn’t, firmly believe that
Sayers beat Heenan, and that Sayers’ friends didn’t rusli
the ring and stop proceedings when they found their man
was hopelessly done for. That belief, however, is as
popular in Australia as the idea that the Prussians had
nothing to do with Napoleon losing Waterloo.

A =£2o a-side post-boring'match lately happened
on Eurongella Station (Thargomindah, Cj., way) between
James Hendry and Arthur Wessel, the posts being from
Sin. upwards in thickness and to be bored for five wires.
Hendry beat Wessel by 172 posts (or 800 holes) against
157 posts (or 785 holes) in 0} hours.

threatens to run cricket hard for popularity this season.
The youngsters are enthusiastic about the Yankee game.

Personal Items.
George Washington’s false teeth are to be
exhibited at Chicago.
Booth is a pawnbroker ! He can’t alienate
himself from the spout.
Sir Henry Parkes, now in his 78th year,
says he will revisit Mudgee 10 years hence.
Lord Salisbury is “ the first Prime Minister
of England who has worn a beard, for over 300
years. ”
Bonython, editor of the S.A. Advertiser*
wears a black frock-coat and belltopper all the
year round.
The Rothschildren have a maxim never to
deal with an unlucky man ; yet they are in with
some in Melbourne.
Is inebriety hereditary ? Turn up the
family history of the next legislator’s son who
hacks at his own larynx.
Railway-Chairman Smith is reckoned to
be the best player of solo-whist in Adelaide.
He gets plenty of practice.
A whale has been seen in the vicinity of
the Victorian Heads lately. Davies surely. We
heard he’d return like a whale.
The ling round the earth, as pictured on
the cover of the Review of Reviews, is, we believe,
Stead’s halo which has slipped down.
The treasurer of the Rockhampton (Q.)
Workers’ Union is named Pinkerton. It just
shows the eternal unfitness of things.
Supreme Court Judge Williams (Vic.) gave
up his bicycle in deference to Mrs. Grundy ; also,
the pulls he used to take on the Yarra.
Toohey, M.P., has a personal grievance
against Sir Dibbs, who, as a Tory knight, is not
likely to drink any more “colonial” beer.
It is again alleged that ex-Governor Sir
William JervoD, who has a station at Wellington
oi the Munay, contemplates entering S.A.
politics.
Ex-Premier Gillies speaks of a presenta-
tion portrait of Speaker Bent (Vic.) as “This
beautiful picture.” Rough on the portrait-
painter !
It is to be hoped that, for the sake of
artistic verisimilitude, Cotton’s friends left Sleath’s
blood and hair clinging to that silver-mounted
bludgeon.
The three leading members of the Holder
(S. A.) Ministry—Holder, Cockburn, and Gordon
—are the same age—42. Handyside is the only
Minister over 50.
Old George Thornton returned to Australia
by last French mailboat —a very different craft
from that in which he first arrived in Australia
with Captain Cook.
Rev. Dr. Bevan (£3O a week) is off soon
on another trip to Chicago Exposition. You see,
his piesence is imperatively demanded, for an im-
portant religious Conference.
M‘ll wraith’s people have been plumbers
in Ayr for nearly 200 years. His father was a
plumber. Heredity ! Ye gods ! what a light is
now cast upon his political career !
Nobody seems to know exactly why Mr.
John Nicholson, S.M., of Port Adelaide, resigned
his position so abruptly, but everyone is certain
that it was not for the reason assigned.
The late A. Hinckley, J.P. of Naracoorte,
S.A., was foreman of the jury which brought in a
verdict that “ the deceased died by the visitation
of God, accelerated by heart-disease.”
Curly Bellew figures as one of the cele-
brities in a recent copy of the Strand Alagazine,
and is pictured in three stages of loveliness.
Curly’s pa was a devil-dodger. Curly isn’t.
A London paper states that Mr. F. W,
Ward, late editor of Sydney D.T., has been
offered the management of a promising N.S.W.
Irrigation scheme. We presume this is Mulgoa.
Speaker Bent, in accepting that oil-paint-
ing of himself from a few devoted admirers, said
he had never done anything of which he felt
ashamed. One might say as much for an alligator
or a steam-roller.
Problem in Rule-of-Three : The shooting
of one clergyman by Thomas Walker, M.L.A,,
has made the said Walker a teetotaller. How
many clergymen would he have to shoot before be
became a Christian ?
Manager Hendry, the blind super, of
North Adelaide (S.A.) Industrial School for the
Blind, can read the time of day by any ordinary
watch with his sensitive fingers. He travels long
journeys Nortli without guide or escort.
Mr. S. D. Phelps, councillor-at-law of
New York, who has been some time in Australia,
prosecuting a trade-mark case, has returned to
America. It was a “treat” to see Mr. Phelps
expectorate the juice of a large “ quid.”
When Governor Norman was up North
last he invited everyone lie met to come and see
him in Brisbane when they were down. They
have been coming ever since in hordes, till they
say the old man begins to age visibly, and to wish
lie were dead.
“ On the occasion of some wedding the late
Robert Lowe began to descant on what he re-
garded as the absurdity of the marriage service.
‘When I married,’ he exclaimed, * all the worldly
goods with which I endowed my wife might have
been carried in a bundle over my shoulder.’ ‘ Ah,
but Robert,’ interjected Mrs. Lowe, ‘there was
your great intellect.’ * I certainly did not endow
you with that, dear,’ was his merciless rejoinder.”
The Eucalypte Lozenges for colds, made by
Colemane and Sons, are unsurpassed.
It is generally supposed that Civil Servants
retire at 60 years of age. How then does it
happen that the N.S.W. Government has just
appointed, to a police-magistrateship, Mr. Henry
Byrnes, of Parramatta, who must be nearly, if
not quite, that age?
Harry Egerton, whilom editor of the
Temoea (N.S.W.) Independent, and whose dead
body was found the other day in the bush, was
one of the most sanguine of gold prospectors.
“My luck’s bad so far, but it’s bound to come
someday.” It has come.

Gladstone is the oldest Prime Minister
who has ever ruled England. “ Old Pam ” ran
him close, being 81. Gladstone is 83. Pitt was
the youngest man to hold the same office ; he was
Premier at 24. Gladstone was within a few days
of 59 when he first assumed the reins.
Apropos that big Melbourne libel-case.
You can’t stamp out a libel-action by making a
man insolvent. The damages which a man
recovers in Court as compensation for libels upon
his fair fame are legally as inalienable as his fair
fame itself, and cannot be attached in bankruptcy.

Thomas Neill Cream,
the alleged English
poisoner, has a brow
that goes back in a steep
slope from his eyes to the
top of his head, which
then goes down in a per-
pendicular line thence to
his shirt collar, his entire
cranium being shaped
very like a triangle with
hair on it. A man with
a head like that generally
gets hanged for artistic
homicide by poison, while
the man whose cranium
is shaped like a bolster
and boils over at the ears
is hanged for brutal
murder with hob-nailed
boots.

Premier Ballance (M.L.) is seriously ill,
and the chances are that Dick Seddon will be
Acting-Premier for the rest of the session. Seddon
is a man of brains, a good speaker, and a reason-
ably good democrat, but he has an aggressive way
about him like Johnel Sullivan, and the fierce
expression in his eye prevents him becoming
generally popular.
There is a consensus of opinion in London
that Munro should long ago have resigned the
Victorian Agent-Generalship. Retention of office
is, under the circumstances, a distinct injury, not
alone to Victoria, but to the whole of
Australia. Up to the Alunro epoch, not a breath
of scandal had assailed the character of any Aus-
tralian Agent-General.
A London correspondent of the Argu s
says that “ the Prince of Wales, by having himself
so constantly photographed with clothes on, has
done much to accelerate democratic and levelling
tendencies.” Democracy and Decency seem to go
hand-in-hand. If Albert Edward were to make a
point of being photographed without his clothes
on, the distinction between the classes and the
masses would be more marked, The Bulletin
supposes.

George Coppin prides himself on being the
only survivor of the Victorian Legislative Council
of 1858, but the hon. George is a mere political
parvenu compared with the octogenarian James
Cowie. For some years past Mr. Cowie has been
the last relic of the original Council which met on
Nov. 6. 1856. Moreover, he was one of the thirty
members of the single-barrelled Legislature that
fired off the Victorian Constitution in 1851.

letter-sound. For instance, “ I have paced again

“ Sir Samuel Wilson,” we read, “is not a
person who cultivates the habit of popularity, and
not only could he have had no chance of being re-
elected for Portsmouth himself but he has made it
impossible for a Conservative to succeed him.”
And as Wilson has been shut out of the Commons,
while the Tories, on whom he has spent so many
thousands, have left office without giving him a
peerage, Sir Salmon may be regarded as having
been absolutely snuffed out. He must live hence-
forth on his dollars and on the reflected radiance
of those who have married some of them.
Some years ago young Tom Icely, as he
was then, having reached adolescence, was sent
abroad to enlarge his mind by travel, after being
specially enjoined by his father to write full des-
criptions of all he saw. Tom did so, in prose and
poetry. Old Mr. Icely was delighted with these
effusions, and when Tom’s muse was inspired by
the sight of the Falls of Niagara the paternal
pride knew no bounds. He button-holed all Tom’s
old acquaintances, and read with pride the
“ pome,” which began thus—mark the utilitarian
idea of the young Australian :
Niagara, Niagara thy falls are veiy steep,
If we had you in Australia what a place for washing
sheep!
The rest was lost in a burst of laughter which papa
Icely never forgave.

newspaper proprietors was Police Inspector L M
thall, who, in 1858, being then only l!) years f
age, started a diminutive sheet at North Shor!
called the Weekly Times. The paper had but a*
ephemeral existence. Lenthall used to humptfi 1
formes each Friday night down to Blue’s Poim
take a ferry to Windmill-street, and carry then’
thence to Clarence-street to be printed. He used t'
write “perishers” on thephinese question, for th°
yellow men were at the time coming in at the rat*
sometimes of 1001) a week, but the great heart of
the people was not to be galvanised then-- it wa-
deprived with prosperity and rum.
Allerdale Grainger, M.P. (S. A.), is a red'
headed six-footer, choke-full from stockings t
collar of virulent invective, but, personally. th°
most kindhearted fellow who ever breathed’ana* 3
themas in the abstract, or denounced an indivi-
dual’s action while willing to befriend the indivi-
dual himself. Like “Tim Linkinwater,” Allerdale
is a perfect lion, a tremendous fellow. Up to date
he is a consistent democrat. He used to edit the
long-defunct Australian Star (Adelaide), when
that weekly was the only South Australian’ paper
that had any real claim to be considered radical
and popular. He was then the stockbutt of the
fat politician with the big bank-balance, but now
he is a dreaded cathartic to the paunchy and
apoplectic.

Professor Marshall Hall, the Melbourne
leg-of-mutton apostle of music, declared in one of
his latest frenzies that “ the works of /Escliylus
and of Melos are as new, as fresh, as those of
Shakespeare, of Angelo, of Wagner.” Whereupon
an inquisitive outsider, with no soul worth men-
tioning, challenged the savant to explain who was
this Melos, and what were his works ? Probably
neither of them knows that Melos was a horse
that ran third for the Melbourne Cup.
Talmage, the American preacher, is of
opinion that every Christian ought to own a fast
horse. “There is,” he says, “a delusion abroad
that a thing must be necessarily good and Christian
if it is slow and dull and plodding. There are
very good people who seem to imagine that it is
humbly pious to drive a spavined, galled, glan-
dered, string-halted, blind, staggered jade. At
the pace some people drive, Elijah, with his horses
of fire, would have taken three weeks to get to
heaven.” This is superfluous. The average
Christian we know always owns a fast horse. Or,
to put it correctly, he owns a slow horse, but he
wallops him so desperately that he has to go fast,
which is just the same thing so far as concerns the
Christian.
The “Death Watch.”
A HOUSEHOLD PEST WHICH MAKES UNCANNY
NOISES.
Who has not heard the “ death watch ?” Many
a person who has happened to be awake in the
stillness of the night has listened to the tick, tick
for half a dozen times or more in succession as
regular and as natural as the ticking of a clock.
Many have regarded the sound with superstitious
awe and dread, because everything about it is so
mysterious. The name death watch is derived in
this way, as a person sitting up with the sick is
more apt to hear such sounds, and when they
were heard at such times, it was considered an ill-
omen to the sick one. Some intrepid person dared
to brave this messenger of death, and found,
instead of the supposed spirit, a guileless, timid
little insect. However, the name is still retained,
and no doubt many still wonder what makes that
peculiar sound and why it is made. It Js wonder-
ful that the mere tick, tick, tick of this harmless
insect should cause such fear and dread in some
people’s mind, who are generally so apathetic in
the matter of the serious signs of approaching
catastrophe, that spitting of phlegm in the morn-
ing, dull aching feeling in the head, eyes, and
back, langour, all tell of the increasing debility of
the system. Recourse must be had to a genuine
restorative which will build up the general
strength and restore the normal health as in the
case of Miss M. Wignall, 46 Fenwick-street, North
Carlton, Melbourne, who writes: “.During the
last seven years I have suffered dreadfully from
neuralgia, passing many sleepless nights of agony
in consequence. So much had the pain interfered
with my general health that I had wasted away to
a shadow, many of my friends thinking I
consumptive. I tried all. kinds of remedies with-
out success until I used Clements Tonic, of which
the first bottle gave me instant relief, and I feel
that Clements Tonic is thoroughly reinvigorating
my whole system, giving me a good appetite and
relish for food, and I have therefore much pleasure
in endorsing the merits of Clements Tonic.”

The full report of
the U.S. Tariff Commis-
sion is even more interest-
ing than the cabled sum-
mary. It sets forth that
since the introduction of
the M’Kinley tariff the
retail prices of 215 articles
of daily consumption have
fallen heavily ; that the
cost of living has gone
down 3.4 per cent., and
that there has been a fall
in wholesale prices all
round. At the same time
the cost of living in Eng-
land has risen 1.9 per
cent.; wages in the U.S.
are now 77 per cent,
higher than in Great
Britain; and American
agricultural produce sold
in Europe has realised
per cent, more than
it did two years ago.
Also, 34 tin-plate works
have been started in the
States since the intro-
duction of the new tariff,
and about 36 which
depended chiefly on the
American trade have
shut up in England.
Which is a pretty good
record for Protection,
taking it all round.

papers to point out that “ the Governor of a self-
governing colony is a Constitutional King.” Well,
Maoriland is pretty tired of governors ; it is tired
of being called a “ colony,” and it is tired of kings;
also, it is exceedingly tired of the adjective “con-
stitutional.”
* ‘ Employers will get cheap labour if they
can; it is the business of the State to prevent
them getting it so cheaply that they imperil the
future of the race by the process; and it is the
business of particular crafts of workmen to sell
their labour at as good a price as they can.”—
Thorold Royers.

Hundreds of white men are stranded
“ stone-broke ” in the big towns of West Austra-
lia, simply because they haven’t got the means to
get themselves out of a province that is being
overrun with Indian coolies and Chinese, engaged
for two years, at wages that would not keep a
white man in even rough tucker.

awhile in N.S. Wales. The Labour-party will
take electoral reform from any hands ; the present
Ministry must give it to them—can give it to them
more speedily than any body that might be
substituted for them. The Upper House dare not
refuse to pass the measure this time.

A sugar-planter sets bis jackeroo to write
to a Southern paper stating that the plantation
Kanakas are as happy as the day is long, and
capital fellows to get on with if you only treat
them as human beings. Every one knows already
that they are capital fellows—sugar-industry-
capital fellows, in fact. Also, if they were only
treated as human beings they wouldn’t be where
they are. No more would the jackeroo.

issue born before marriage of parents subsequently
married.” Why not introduce a Bill to legiti-
matise everybody and to make a man’s goods
equally divisible among all his children at his
death ? The doctrine of “ illegitimacy ”is a relic
of barbarism.

The Young Victorian Patriotic League,
explains the Argus in its summary for Europe,
“ was formed prior to the elections to 'promote the
restoration qf prosperity?’ Europe will be further
interested to learn that this great project was
started by a stockbroker, and that the League is
not itself likely to .contribute anything better than
scrip and skite to the national prosperity.
Judge Boucaut (S.-A.), now in England, is
apparently falling over himself in the effort to
secure a bauble. This erstwhile democratic
Premier thus writes in an English paper : '• „
My pride is in an Imperial connection, and if the
English desert their Imperialism and become dreamers,
doctrinaires, and faddists, then I should say that the
sooner we colonists also separate the better. For my part,
I should feel ashamed of my country, and would no longer
have any objection to a separation.
Jawbone Neild will introduce to the
N.S.W. Legislative Assembly, a Bill for the
suppression of vice; 0. O. Dangar lias one for
the suppression of barmaids, and Kidd one for
the suppression of goats—we mean drunkenness.
This is piecemeal legislation. Somebody should
grapple boldly with the situation, and introduce a
Bill for the suppression of sin.
“ England alone gives a majority of 71
against Gladstone,” crows the Argus. But Scot-
land supplies a larger proportionate majority in
favour of Home Rule, and the Argus never misses
a chance of remarking on the superior wisdom of
the “hard-headed Scot.” In point of fact, the
entire anti-Horae Rule press of Australia is
always accusing itself of imbecility in opposing
the principles of the more enlightened portion of
the United Kingdom. Make a note of this.
A Queensland magistrate, who recently
sentenced two unionists to terms of imprisonment,
refused to take bail, pooh-poohed appeal, and
declined to certify to the correctness of the copies
of the depositions which their solicitor had
obtained. The Australian Labour Federation
took counsel’s opinion and started certain legal
proceedings to force an appeal. Then the magis-
trate certified to the depositions in a sudden
fright. But the matter has by no means ended.
Bananaland Parliament lias passed a Bill
to enable the Supreme Court Judges of any other
Australian province to adjudicate oh the Queens-
land Bench. The other provinces should now pass
reciprocal Acts, thus adding to the volume of
desirable federal legislation. By-and-bye, the
foreign Privy Council may be dropped down the
worked-out shaft of colonial dependency, and the
Appeal Court of the Republic be made the final
umpire of Australian litigation.
The official statements (cabled) of Presi-
dent Hodge, of the Glasgow Trades Union Con-
gress, which statements are ail cautiously con-
servative, and would maintain the expected,
respectful, touch-your-cap-to-the-squire and salute-
the-baiiie attitude of labour, are enthusiastically
approbated by the S.M. Herald as exhaling “a
breezy vigour.” The vigour of the resigned slave,
of the recreant blackleg, of the toady special con-
stable, and of the National Ass. groom.
Mr. T. Buckland represented the N.S.W.
National Ass. with much accuracy, the other day,
when, on a deputation to the Government, anent
a Local Government Bill, he hoped that the voting
power would be left in the hands of the property-
owners, and not shared by those who “ received
benefit in the shape of wages.” The same old
bray, that the man who receives benefit in the
shape of work done is a benefactor when he hands
to the workman half the value of the work and
pockets the other half himself.
The Griffilwraith Cabinet decided that it
could not, “from motives of humanity,” drive
back into the dry and howling desert those Chows
who came over the Queensland border without
paying the poll-tax. Therefore, they are coming
in by droves “from motives of humanity,” and
already the squatters are employing them at nine-
pence a day and their rice, and sacking the white
men to make room for them. When the Griffil-
wraith Cabinet shows the slightest sign of
humanity then it is time for the country to throw
a brick at it without further consideration.
Humanity is an unnatural emotion with the
Kanaka Government, and is always an evidence
that there is trouble ahead.
Griffith’s Kanaka regulations have roused
into rebellion a number of Polynesians who, under
the old law, were known as “time-expired ” boys)
and who had recognised labour rights in common
with white men, but who are now classed with
the “slaves.” (For The Bulletin repeats that
any section of workers who are legislatively for-
bidden to do any but one class of work are
“slaves” pure and simple, and “slavery” is
illegal within British dominions). Twenty Kana-
kas, 10 of whom have been 15 years, and the rest
nine years, in Queensland, have applied to the
Bowen police-magistrate for tickets exempting
them from being classed as Polynesians under
the slavery regulations. The matter has been
referred to Chief Slaver Griffith.
The D.N. Argus (Rockhampton, Q.),
which sooled on the Tory dogs against the unionist
shearers last year, throws back its ears over the
“latest act of tyranny” perpetrated against
Central Queensland by Griffilwraith and Co. “in
proposing to remove the lightship in Keppel Bay ” (!),
and trusts that it will—
Still further unite all classes in the community to the
resistance of tyranny by every constitutional means at
first, nut, if these fail, then we must take by force what
we cannot get in any other way. . . . We must pro-
test to the Government against this proposed outrageous
piece of oppression, but if they will not hear us by “fair”
means, then let us make them hear us by “foul,” and
God help the right!
Now, then, Judge Harding ! Forward with the
Gatlings, good Towzer !
The shades of night were falling fast,
When thro’ a Yankee city passed
A knight, who maddened by a bore,
The cable says, he up and swore.
(Of course you’ve read it.)
Now in New Zealand far away
When interviewed the other day,
That self-same cable makes him say
“ I never said it! ”
A Funny Game.
A few friends met the other night in a Newtown
store to spend a jovial evening, but as they were
all teetotallers and none of them drank anything
stronger than Clements Tonic, drunkenness was
out of the question. Mac. proposed a game
at euchre, and Jones said they had better play for
something just to give the game an interest and
make it lively, but times were hard, so they did
not care to play for money. Smith, however,
spied a box of Fletcher's bilious and liver pills,
and proposed that each time a man was beaten he
should take a pill, and this made the game lively
and the fun was fast and furious. Simpson
swallowed five, Jones got away with four and
Smith and Hopkins two eacli and Mac. got off
scot free. The game terminated by a general
exodus at 3 a.m., for Dr. Fletcher’s pills never
labour in vain. They are the best and mildest
pills made and are sold by all chemists and
druggists everywhere; being a positive, safe,
and reliable cure for constipation, liver complaint,
headache, &c. They always give satisfaction.
The proprietors give away £5O (in seven prizes) at
Xmas, for the seven first nearest guesses sent in,
as to how many of these pills a large Clements
Tonic bottle (now full) holds. A guess form is
around every box, and all such forms are eligible
for this competition that come from around a box.
One box of Fletcher’s Pills proves their virtues
and they can be obtained everywhere at Is., or
post free 13 penny stamps, from E. M. Clements,
Newtown, N.S.W.

PE HELL’S
WHEAT
MEAL.
Sociely
The other day some Sydney bank-boomers
were locked up pending bail. By-and-hye they
felt hungry, and as their friends had forgotten to
send them in dinner they began to feel interested
in the plates of hominy which were shoved into
their cell. “ Don’t like the look of it much,” said
one to the other, “ hut I’ll see what it’s like. 11l
scrape all the sugar on to one side and try it that
way ” —which he did. He took a big mouthful.
Ugh! The “ sugar ” turned out to he salt. P.S.
This occurrence strikes us as being allegorical. A
lot of people have lately gone for the “ sugar ” and
found it to be salt after all.
• « #
“When women first found out that they
wanted to become doctors,” writes a well-informed
correspondent of The Bulletin, “ the great argu-
ment in their favour was the advantage other
women would gain from being attended by practi-
tioners of their own sex in those delicate ailments
which it is assumed that they cannot describe to
men without sacrificing their modesty. Now,
however, it turns out that it is the modesty of the
men that is in danger. The female students at
Melbourne Hospital cannot be induced to pay any
attention to the cases of the countless women who
come as outdoor patients, and rarely show them-
selves in the ward where these applicants are re-
ceived. Bit when the diseases of the men are
being investigated the whole dozen now studying
in the hospital flock into the room with benevo-
lent activity. The larrikin who cannot find the
guinea for L. L. Smith can be sure of an attentive
and interested audience when he explains how ‘ he
fell over a doorstep ’ to the surgeon of the day and
his fair assistants. The female patients, on their
part, will have nothing to do with the ‘ doctresses.’
The truth is that nature is too strong for the
theorists.”
* * «
The S.A. Land Boards in all save very
exceptional cases decline to grant selections to
bachelors. Gastine, M.P., objects to this, and, in
arguing on the subject last week, said : “Youths
must make a home for themselves before they get
married, and must be married before they have
large families.”
m * m
A Tasmanian woman drank fifteen quarts
of beer prior to a certain interesting event, and
one quart immediately afterwards. She died.
* * #
Depression has got its grip on a lot of
Marvellous Melbourne clubs, and members find
that belonging to them is as had as being a land-
bank shareholder. Bohemians have to face a call
of about £3O a member; Nomads one of about £6;
the squatters at the Australian about £lOO each ;
while the Yorick is racked with internal sickness,
the Athenaeum is gasping, and the marble halls of
the Melbourne are desolate, save for the cry of the
new-chum, “ Witer ! kill me a kib ! ” five minutes
after arrival. They have all boomed, and now
they are all busting.
The Brisbane press speaks of the Queens*,
land Investment “ cases ” now under appeal as
though there were no others remaining. The cash
just tried is only a preliminary canter. Another
action against the same four defendants is set
down for a claim of £6OOO in connection with a
loan to the Sandgate Pier Co; also for £6OOO re
a loan to L. F. Sachs ; also for £20,000 re a loan
to Rochefort Bros., and for loans amounting to
£lOO,OOO to Thos. Hamer. Lastly, there are two
actions set down against two directors who are
also defendants in all the preceding cases. All
the amounts claimed carry interest, which extends
in some cases over a period of many years. Why,
the case just tried is a mere flea-bite !
*’ * «
The final judgment in the last of the
mortgage-company cases aforesaid will probably be
delivered at the fin de sieclc after next.

An insolvent and the man who “put him
through ” were responsible for two of the toniest
and best-advertised theatre-parties during the
Gaiety Co.’s Brisbane season. What was more,
they came off together, and were placed so that a
couple of big blankets could have covered the lot.

A prominent Melbourne attorney has
Bulwerish capillary variations. His hair has been
white, black and golden, and now he goes in for
white hair with black moustache.
* * #
The Sunday morning services at Melbourne
Anglican Cathedral are now under vice-regal
patronage, and the “plate” is said to be doing
better business in consequence. The silver “ bob”
of respectability begins to supersede the bent half-
penny and the metal trousers-button of the taber-
nacle’s earlier days. Government House can
always administer a fillip to Christianity in this
way.
# * *
“A Disciple of Howard” writes that he
believes there is a man in Pentridge doing 10
years for sacrilege. He has done a fair three years
for stealing tin-pots, or something, but has the
rest of the “ stretch ” because they were taken out
of the Judge’s church.
# # #
At Parramatta (N.S.W.), months ago, two
men were charged with conspiracy and admitted
to bail. One ran away ; the other pleaded guilty
and endured six mouths’ gaol. On his liberation,
he found his mate and “ blew the gaff ” on him.
No. 1 was then re-arrested, tried, and—acquitted.
Now, with whom did No. 2 conspire?

Apropos Sydney’s recent big and gorgeous
£lOOO Chinese funeral. The other day in Maori-
land a Chinaman died and went to be with
his own particular brown-paper deity in a land
where there are many, mansions, most of them
being built out of old tea-oliests. He left about
£l5O worth of assets, and instructed a yellow
executor to realise the estate, and use it in for-
warding the deceased to his old Asiatic home,
and supplying him with a decent funeral. There-
upon the executor set to work to consider how
much he could save out of the property, and as the
freight on corpses was high he decided to send the
departed home on a cheaper basis. And this was
why a Maoriland shipping company was horrified
one morning at the conduct of a Chinaman who
came in and said : “Me wantee know how much
you fleeze belly poor Chineeman an’ send him
back home longa meat to Hong Kong in fleezing
THE LOST PUB.
MAN FROM JAMBEROO (to constable): "Where is the pub. I stay at?"
CONSTARLE : “What’s the name of it?”
MAN FROM JAMBEROO : “Dunno. ’Fore I left home my wife wrote down the directions on a
bit o’ paper and I gave it to the man as drove me in a eab from the railway. I
come out this mornin’ after breakfast and I’ve lost the run of the darn place.
S’pose I’ll ’ave to write to the old woman and ask her where I’m stoppin’.”
ship, sposee you fleeze ’im allee same mutton,
chargee allee same, save twelve pong ten for lead
koffing, ’im belly poo’, they belly poo’, all of us
belly poo’.” But the company decided that
Chinaman wasn’t legitimate meat, and when that
ship reached Hong Kong there was no frozen
heathen hanging up among the mutton, and an
unburied Mongol still cries aloud for sepul-
ture.

It took three juries ere a verdict could be
obtained against three Melbourne men for robbing
the Albert Park railway-station. The Crown
would save a lot of wear and tear if it tried these
cases with a jury of policemen.
* # *
Some swamp-poet of Marrickville has
suggested that the name of the railway-station
there should he changed to Tramvale, and that
aerial and gossamer-like mind which rules the
N.S. Wales Public Works Department has cheer-
fully acquiesced. There are lots of better names,
just as elegant and aesthetic, which might have
been chosen instead of Tramvale. Stoke-hole, for
example, or Brake-downs, or Shunt-off, or Pas-
senger-dump. Or the name of the Minister might
have been gracefully memorised in combination
with the utilitarian aspect sought to be preserved
in “ Tram ’’-vale. Why not Tender Lyne ? That
should meet with the approval of every man with
a steak in the country.

# # *
A correspondent tells us this story : A
veteran Sydney constable was sent down to do
night-duty on Spectacle Island. The dismal
loneliness of the place conduced to melancholia,
during a fit of which the officer cut his throat.
When the man was charged with attempted
suicide, the presiding magistrate mercifully
ordered him to be imprisoned until the rising of
the court, which nominal sentence was probably in
view of the poor policeman’s official future. How-
ever, the unfortunate constable, after 30 years of
approved service in the force, has been dismissed
and made to forfeit the pension to which he had
become, or almost become, entitled by that long
period of well-ordered behaviour. Is that one
lapse, obviously the result of mental derangement,
to he used as a pretext for cancelling more than
half a lifetime of disciplined conformity to the
requirements of the service? It would be as well
if some member of Parliament moved for the
papers in this case.
* • •
Mayor Bullock, of Adelaide, omitted from
the list of invitations to his recent ball the names
of every Labour M.P., except Mr. Charleston.
One of the gentlemen thus ignored represents the
Assembly district in which the Town Hall is
situated, while another sits for the central pro-
vince in the Legislative Council. Bullock also
left out the names of the President of the Trades
and Labour Council and the Chairman of the
Eight Hours Celebration Union —who have always
been invited in the past —and yet had the cool
effrontery to join the Vice-Regal party which
attended the luncheon given by the Executive at
the Eight-Hours demonstration the day after the
ball.

Editor Bulletin. —Sir, From Sydney
Echo
The Wolverhampton Guardians evidently take good
care that the nerves of the paupers are not weakened by
the consumption of strong tea. At the meeting of the
board the other day it was mentioned that the price paid
for tea for the inmates of the workhouse was Is. 2d. per
lb., and that 10 pints were made out of 1 oz.! The state-
ment was received with laughter.
What is there to laugh at ? On the crack Austra-
lian stations, owned by honourables from Scot-
land and syndicates from Sydney, the old “ 10,10,
two-and-a-quarter ” are still adhered to. Now, a
boundary-rider makes a quart of tea three times a
day, 42 pints a week, and that is often hulled ft r a
drink between meals. As to the price, the best
ration tea I ever tasted was at Merangle— price,
lid. per lb. in Sydney. At present I am using the
worst tea manufactured (I use this word advisedly),
but I do not know the price, because the boss
refuses to let the_ shearers know, until near the
cut-out, when it is too late to bark. Add to it
that the water is from a 12 by 12 tank in a three-
acre paddock, in which they had 50 used-up, foot-
rotty ranis for three weeks before shearing, and I
fail to see why my statement should not also be
“received with laughter.”—Yours, Scotty W.

society, rny dear.

Two Jaypees, a solicitor, and a trooper
are holding a carouse in a N.S.W. country pub-
parlour. A drunken bushman rides past the
window. Trooper goes out and runs him in. The
Jaypees try him for drunkenness and promptly
fine him <£lo. “ Damn it {hie), I’ve only got ss. on
me; I’ll have ter take it out.” Solicitor rises and
addresses the “Court” as “ amicus cur ice” Pine
promptly reduced to ss. Exit grateful bushman.
“ Fill ’em up agen !”

Coincidently with cholera in England, the
price of disinfectants (it is cabled) has increased
about 200 per cent. What sort of a “corner”
will now be rigged by the wholesale importing
druggists of this hemisphere? Even the oppres-
sive pub-owning and brewing monopolies do not
transcend in overmastering magnitude the whole-
sale-drug octopi.

You have only to notice the wife of a
chicken-hearted Northern lawyer-politician to
understand that whatever pluck he has is not his
own—but borrowed.
* * *
The fussiest judges in Australia, in the
matter of man-millinery, are the Supreme Court
Judges of Queensland. Their “scarlet-robes”
days are quite social functions, and regularly
receive the advertising approval of the flunkeys of
the Brisbane daily press.

Judge Wilkinson to a Parramatta jury:
“ The prosecutor, gentlemen, drank three glasses
of beer during the afternoon, I don’t know what
effect this would have upon him ; but you, gentle-
men, will no doubt be able to arrive at a proper
conclusion on the subject.”
# * #
Chief Justice Way (S.A.), chatting of his
U.S. travels :
The State of Massachusetts has the ■ highest reputation
in the States for the administration of justice. I heard a
case tried in which the leading counsel on one side was a
lady, while her junior was her husband. The junior cer
tainly seemed the most humble man in court.
Way himself is a bachelor and can safely jeer at
henpeckdom.
# # *
The Manawatu Standard (M.L.) tells of
a “ widow lady of education and refinement” who,
with her two unmarried daughters, took up a
selection in preference to falling back on the
genteel drudgery of a governess’s existence. They
built their own house, cleared, stumped and fenced
the land almost entirely with their own hands,
and are now raising wheat and potatoes with con-
siderable success. They are a plucky trio, and
deserve good fortune, but all the same society
looks coldly on the lady who gets up early 7 on a
wet morning to urge the plough through the
slush, with her skirts turned up to her knees, and
it will probably continue doing so to the end of
the chapter.
* * #
Before the North Cross Reef, at Stawell,
turned out so richly, an individual who is now
a full-blown “Honourable,” but was then
known only as “Bill the Smelter,” was not
one of the richest men in Victoria, as he sub-
sequently became. He owned 2125 shares out of
the 10,000 forming the co., and the calls were a
heavy tax on him. But he had the credit apart
from this of being one of the most “ thrifty ” of
men, and used to live by himself away down at
Doctor’s Creek, and cook his own food. No o®'
had ever shared his dinners or knew what be lived
on. One night he was at a meeting of directors
in a Patrick-street pub. and bung a bag rap in the
passage. Very soon it was noticed* and th®
loungers in the bar resolved that for once they
would find out what Bill lived on. Th«y opened
the bag quickly, and out tumbled or rolled »
couple of podgy little bull pups, just old enough to
be weaned. To thisday there are several malevolent
rascals who significantly say that the pups were
fat and toothsome-looking, and that they were
never seen at maturity in their then owners
company.
THE GAIETY CO.
Not for thy love, oh ! bewchus maid,
The modern suitor humbly begs;
He asks not grace, nor charm of face—
His soul bows down before thy legs.
When from the front-row stalls, the sigh,
The passioned exclamation, slips,
The baldheads’ glare, the Johnnies’ stare
Are turned on Addie Conyers’ hips.
The fame of her whose modelled shanks,
Formed of the dandy’s gems a part,
Grows dim ’gainst thine ; thy limbs divine
Are treasured in each chappie’s heart.

When are 'Spectacles Kcqnlrcd ?
When the eyes water or become so much fatigued by
short exercise ns to he obliged to he frequently closed or
relieved by viewing different objects.
When objects cannot he seen without removing them
to an increased distance.
When the letters of a book appear to blend with one
anol her.
When more light is necessary than was formerly, as
When the candle is placed between the eye and the
book ;
When much difficulty is found in threading a needle ;
When the eyes appear as if they had a mist before
them;
When black specks seem floating in the sight.
When any of the above indications arise, all affectation
should be laid aside, responsible and skilful opticians con-
sulted, and a pair of preservers purchased.

* » •
A portent from Maoriland. At Maunga-
kawa, recently, a Maori woman gave birth to
triplets. The mother and two of the children
died immediately afterwards; but the third child,
an unfinished male infant who was born without
hands, is alive and doing well. The solemn
patriarchs of the tribe have sat in judgment upon
this prodigy, and their decision is that the infant
is without hands so that he cannot sign away his
inheritance to the Pakeha, and his existence is a
warning that the Maori is to hang on to the land
for ever.
# * *
Chief Justice Way (S.A.), the individual
who had the dog given him by Lady Kintore, held
forth the other evening before the Adelaide
P.M.C.A. on his recent travels in “Yurrup,”
Kintore being in the chair. It was a remember-
some event. Way’s “ travels ” consisted chiefly of
a catalogue of the emperors and other potentates
He had seen afar off, and every time he came to a
fresh emperor his voice fell into an awe-stricken
whisper, and when he had mentioned that emperor
he made an impressive pause so that the monarch
might have time to soak into the minds of his.
audience. Bub the very saddest part of the narra-
tion was when he stated, with crape om
his voice, that he saw the Princess of Wales,
dancing with the Duke of Clarence, while
a number of other dukes looked on and whole,
rafts of earls and barons were about; and on that
memorable occasion (here Way’s voice sank to a
husky murmur like a hog breathing afar off)
nothing happened to convey the impression that
Clarence was shortly going to die. Every time
the speaker alluded to anybody with a title the.
audience gave a wild and unanimous cheer, and
when he mentioned the disease that Clarence died
°f the applause was tremendous. It doesn’t
sound well to hear an audience cheering for a fatal
complaint, but doubtless the misguided crowd,
meant well.
* # *
Governor Glasgow struck a prize groveller
at a recent ball in Wellington (M.L.). He was am*
auctioneer, and being introduced to Glasgow he
confided to that dignitary, in a husky whisper,
that he was “ only a rough colonial,” but he hoped
his Excellency would overlook the fact. His Ex-
cellency promised to overlook it. The auctioneer
further stated that he “ kep’ a diary,” and his
Excellency expressed himself gratified but some-
what bored at the circumstance. Then the rough
colonial reached up to Glasgow’s manly red ear,
and asked, in a profound whisper, if the Governor
would mind him recording in that diary that he
had enjoyed the blessed privilege of conversing
with the Queen’s representative, and the Viceroy
told him that he wouldn’t mind it in the least.
The scene closed with a glad auctioneer rushing
for home to make the necessary entry while the
circumstances were still fresh in his so-called
mind.

Parson Webb (Q.), who deplores the un-
righteousness of the bushman across the range,
never heard of Mitchell. It has more churches
than pubs., and more parsons than churches. Its
chief attraction is a Wesleyan church painted
pink.
• * *
On the Fly River, New Guinea, commerce
is still in the “grip for grip ” stage. The barterers
grip each other’s barter, and exchange by letting
go their holds together. Civilisation employs
police to do its gripping—afterwards!
# # #
At a Brisbane police court the other day a
sly-grog case in a well-known shady shop was
being heard. For the defence elaborate plans of
the premises were introduced. “These,” said
Murray, the P.M, “ are not necessary. The house
is well-known to the magistrates on the bench ! ”
Then there was a solemn stillness broken only by
the sly, faraway smile of the foorce.

Some well - advertised cancer-remedies,
according to the English Medical Journal, have
been analysed, and consist entirely of pure water.
This doesn’t absolutely prove, however, that they
won’t cure cancer—in fact, water properly applied
assuredly will do so. The present writer knew
of a case in Maoriland when a hot, reckless,
fat man took his clothes off and stepped under
the stream, or rather the waterfall, which ran the
POLO MADE USEFUL-SETTLING THE RABBITS IN RIVERINA.
wheel of a big battery, and as the fluid had a
•clear dm®p of about 100 feet it just struck him on
the head once and abolished him. He didn’t have
•cancer, tat if he had it is quite obvious that he
'wouldnlt have had it any more.

• • *
Beyond Dubho (N.S.W.), small-change
consists of kangaroo-scalps and pigs’-snouts
(according to a Dispatch man). The Stock and
Pastures Board gives 6d. for a scalp and Is. for a
snout aforesaid. You want a drink ? Down goes
a scalp. You “shout” for three? Down go a snout
and a scalp; or two snouts, and you get a scalp as
change. Nothing is more simple.
* # *
The religious ceremonial portion of Free-
masonry seems to be free from the Tory taint that
gives such an unpleasant odour to the internal
arrangements of the Craft. Two programmes of
Masonic services, held in Melbourne recently,
show a wholesome contempt for credal distinctions.
At St. Kilda Town Hall, for instance, the pre-
centor was a reverend Hebrew, the organist and
musical conductor represented Low and High
Church of England respectively, whilst the
sermon was preached by that plump Congrega-
tional cherub, Brother Bevan. Also, it was a
Wesleyan mayor who paid for the holy show.
A Nowra (N.S.W.) cornstalk, much
addicted to cursing, was once riding alone - an ugly
track with Rev. Mr. Dobbie. Asthe fearful oaths
emerged from the mouth of his unregenerate com-
panion, Dobbie felt down deep into a pocket for a
tract against swearing that he knew he had on him
somewhere. “Young man,” said he, suddenly,
“are you prepared to die?” The young fellow
turned, saw his stern-faced comrade with one of
his hands feeling suggestively in concealment, dug
in his spurs, and galloped off to a safe distance.
Then he shouted back: “Shoot now, you (bally-
bugcrusted) old bushranger, and be damned to you!
I don’t believe you could hit a (goggledamper)
bullock-team, (dogmast) your (bally) eyes !”
There is at present on view in Sydney a
picture that deserves more than a passing notice,
not only on account of its intrinsic artistic merits,
but because it deals with a subject distinctly Aus-
tralian. The painter, Mr. L. J. Steele, an English
artist resident in Maoriland, has sent this work
over for exhibition as a preliminary to his settling
in N.S.W. The scene depicted is “The Last
Stand of Captain Starlight,” and is a dramatic
representation of the final incident in Rolf
Boldrewood’s book, “Robbery Under Arms.”
The centre of the composition is occupied by Star-
light and his horse Rainbow, the bushranger being
in the act of firing at the approaching trooper,
who is out of the picture. The dead body of Jim
Marston lies in the foreground on the left, half
within the shadow of a gum-tree, while his brother,
Dick, is represented on the other side, in the
middle distance, powerless to help his leader,
having been placed out of the fight by a bullet
that has broken his right arm. In the rear two
troopers, under the direction of the superintendent
of Police, are executing a flank movement so as to
ensure the death or capture of Starlight. The
subject was one that presented many difficulties,
but Mr. Steele has surmounted them, and has
produced a very striking picture. Every detail
has been carefully studied and most faithfully
painted, and nothing could be finer than the draw-
ing of the foreshortened body of Jim Marston,
with the hands stretched out in the dying attempt
to grasp a revolver just out of his reach. The
figures of Starlight and his horse are admirably
posed, the expression of dogged determination on
the face of the cornered bushranger being particu-
larly well done. To those who are accustomed to
the so-called “broad ” work of many of our local
artists, the conscientious carefulness and high
finish of Mr. Steele may appear hard, but these
qualities are often great artistic merits.
THE LIFE-BOAT MAN.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
„ me for my pluck, mate, I was born upon the
pon t pi alBe
, s f a ’>Un to man the life-boat comes quite natural to

, sor t o’ deadly peril in the work, I must allow,

Bllt »dav i ma/he drownded, that’s a fate we all expecks,
Though we’ve had a lot o’ practice in a-going out to

careful sir, as does it; when a vessel’s on a reef
We wait until we’re certain that she’s fairly come to
Stl* sea, God ’elp ’em 1 is a-runnin’ extry high,
We signals that assistance will be ready by-and-bye.
, or yer S ee, unless a steamer comes to take our boat in
It’s impossible to launch her when the stormy winds do
« oftentimes it’s happened that we sat upon the sands
While a noble craft was sinking-simply wringing of our
’ands.
you can fancy what’s our feelings and imagine our
regret *
still, ft’s best to stop ashore, sir, if you’ve got to be
Now” I’d sooner save a crew than hack the winner of the
But you can’t do deeds of valour in a boat that’s bottom-
itp.
Some thinks we ought to risk it, so them papers seem to
But the’dooty of a life-boat ain’t to chuck a chance away,
And we never put her off, sir, till we know we can arrive
At our awful destination as we ought to do—alive.
Bad luck to dirty weather! You’ll have noticed, boss,
ThaUfaccidents don’t ’appen when the sea’s as smooth
as glass.
The vessels goes to pieces, and the victims has to drown,
Before the ragin’ waters gets a chance to settle down.
We nearly saved some folks, a dozen year ago, or more :
A brig, as I remember, struck quite ’andy to the shore ;
It wasn’t far to pull, so, after waitin’ for a while,
Our captin says, “ Let's go, hoys!” and we went away in
style.
My word ! that teas a night, mate, as I never shall forget,
Before we saw the danger every man of us was wet;
The spray splashed in our faces if we stopped to have a
drink,
And we hadn’t quite got there before the brig began to
sink.
She vanished, more’s the pity! and we rowed around in
vain,
And saw no livin’ creature, ’cept ourselves, upon the
main,
But we found a baby’s body, ay, and brought it home all
right—
My ’cart leaps when 1 think, sir, of that memorable night.
The little thing lies buried to the left of Blackman Cave,
And once a year we hold a kind of meetin’ at the grave ;
The capt’in makes a speech, and then they calls on me to
“shout” —
For I first saw that baby, and ’twas me as pulled it out.
“God bless the brave old life-boat /” You may well say
that, indeed:
She is always true and willin’ in the solemn hour of
need:
And well deserves her title ; she may pitch a bit and roll,
But all the years I’ve known her, why, she hasn’t lost a
soul!
Melb.

Bright’s
Disease
Cured.
aREAT success is being scored in Sydney
with the Aubert Remedies. They have
really caught on well with our New South Wales
neighbours, who proclaim them a first-rate cure
for the various ills that frail mankind.is heir to.
Of course, when a medicine has attained the
wide popularity these medicines have gained for
themselves, there are bound to be a few dissentient
voices; it is the penalty of success, as it is of
greatness. But confident that the majority of
those who have given them a fair trial are
thoroughly satisfied with the result of their experi-
ment, the advice to one and all is, when they feel
that formidable enemy to application to duty,
cheerfulness of disposition, &e., a severe cold
coming on, to try Marupa. If the first dose does
not relieve, the second will.
K ir *dly. note the four Remedies, Marupa,
1 aramo, Karana and Natanata, each of which are
invaluable in their turn.
Karana as a Tonic cannot be surpassed, while
Paramo has now proved itself to be a cure for
Bright’s Disease. In a letter lately received from
Mother Mary Joseph Aubert, she states she
effected a perfect cure with it. The patient went
to her as a last resourse, for doctors had given
him up ; he was swollen in the face with the effect
of the disease. However, the worthy lady took
him in hand, and gave him a half teaspoonful of
Paramo every morning, and the same dose every
second evening.
After three weeks of that treatment he went
away perfectly restored to health.
This instance should prove how valuable these
herbal remedies are likely to become when they
were able to cure an affliction like Bright’s Disease
m its last stage.
Nothing Succeeds Like Success.
Three quarters of a year has now passed since
the AUBERT REMEDIES were first made
public, and it certainly can be now said that they
have earned a splendid reputation. As cures for
the several ills they are recommended for, there
can be no doubt of their good character. All
medicines put on a market must be reliable, be-
cause it matters not how much you advertise if
there are not the best qualities to work with, your
money will be ill spent and bring in no just return.
The Remedies under notice have conclusively
proved themselves to be sterling, otherwise the
sales would never have reached their present pro-
portions, nor would such valuable testimony have
been received as is now in the possession of the
proprietors.
In New South WaSes, where they are now being
introduced, a steady demand has already set in,
helped in a great measure by the evidence of
success in New Zealand.
“KARANA
Is a very favourite medicine, owing to its “tonic
and fortifying nature,” as testified by LORD
ONSLOW. It is quite harmless in its action, but
it is sure with good result, and must give satisfac-
tion to such persons as will give it a fair trial.
Most persons have an idea that one bottle of
medicine will cure their complaints, and if they
do not feel quite well after taking two or three
doses they give the Remedy up in disgust. Pro-
bably these people have gone on SUFFERING
FOR YEARS, and then, because they see an
article advertised which EXACTLY FITS
THEIR COMPLAINT, they feel they ought to
give it a trial; but what does this trial consist of ?
Merely a dose or two, and if in a day a cure is not
effected on a complaint which probably has taken
years to build up, they say the medicine is worth-
less and not worth bothering with. Few persons
like to accept good advice, but to one and all we
say, if our Remedy is,worth trying at all, let it at
least have a fair chance. A broken down con-
stitution cannot be built up again in a day, there-
fore we emphatically say that if
“KARANA”
is persevered with it will with everybody merit
the reputation it now has as one of the BEST
TONICS IN EXISTENCE.

value of which no one can gainsay.

William Scarrah was, the other day’
charged before the local Acting-Magistrate at
Fremantle, W.A., with having assaulted Ellen
Leurington, a married aborigine. From the West
Australian
The Bench, after hearing the address for the defence,
said that there was not sufficient evidence upon which to
send accused for trial, and therefore he would dismiss
the information ; but, as he had no doubt that the offence
had been committed, and in view of the very bad record
of the accused, he would send him to gaol for two years,
with hard labour (11!)
The correspondent who forwards this extract
adds “ This is not a misprint, but a solid fact,
as I found out by enquiry.”
In a Baltimore (U.S.A,) cemetery lie the
remains of a man and his two wives. The man’ B
grave is in the centre, and he has a wife on either
side of him. On each lady’s tombstone is a hand
■which once pointed towards the husband’s burial
place, with the inscription attached: “I was
his.” On the husband’s grave are two hands
pointing to the remains of the two wives, with the
brief remark ; “ They were mine.” But time and
the irony of fate have slewed round the slab over
the younger of the two wives, and the hand above
her dust now points towards a deceased Italian
fruit-vendor with the statement, “I was his,”
while through the sinkage of her tombstone the
warning finger on the husband’s slab is directed
over her head towards a defunct actress, There
is probably a great, neglected truth in that
cemetery.

bv a grey, once by a black colt, twice by chestnuts, ten
times'by browns, and fourteen times by bays. One grey

There was big excitement at “ the Park,”
Sydney, last week over the Trot. Silver Cloud was sent
out to win, and Blott was put up in the sulky behind her.
He set sail, and soon passed everything but the flying
pacer, Isabel, and just as Cloud looked like going by the
little mare, an old woman deliberately made a pass at her
with an open parachute, and the Cloud broke up. The
croud, who have backed the Cloud times out of number,
seemed furious at the prospect of her winning when they
were “ not on,” and pelted her with clods of clav, and
other missiles, and a big demonstration was made when
Isabel won. Blott, after the race, said that so far from
being disappointed at not winning, he felt overjoyed at
escaping with his life.

As no N.S.W. politician has so many
personal friends as has Dibbs, a very warm
welcome was extended by them on his return to
Sydney last Tuesday by the American mail. The
injury that Dibbs’ reputation for sturdy independ-
ence has sustained by the fact that he departed
as a clearskin and avowed Republican and
"returned as a branded knight, is obtrusively
apparent in the desperate efforts of his special
champions to invent excuses for his pitiable fall.
Lie most common of these is that Dibbs under-
went the surgical oneration of knighthood with
sorrowful fortitude as a heroic means of raising
his country's credit —saving it, in fact, from insol-
vency. The political ideal pictured is the prosti-
tution of a child to save the life of a mother. It
18 too far-fetched in any case. And, if it were
n °t, it is an apotheosis of the meanest kind of
•cowardice inverted so as to appear as in the guise
■of godlike heroism. The genuine atoms of the
Republic (living, though unorganised) will con-
Ltiue to hoot every sham Republican like Dibbs.
The Government in their endeavours to
keep the peace at Broken Hill have apparently
been offering inducements to burglars and in-
cendiaries in Sydney robbing Peter to pay Paul.
About 320 police have been dispatched to Broken
Rill, of whom 280 have been sent from Sydney
and suburbs. The metropolitan division numbers
”00 men all told, so that nearly one-half Sydney’s
Police are at Broken Hill. We strongly advise
"he business and private public to keep valuables
ln the Sydney Safe Deposit.*

Rev. Mr. Morris, of Tam worth (N.S. W.), wants
to discount the luck of original Broken Hill Proprietary
shareholders by asking people to remember the thousands
of pounds sunk in other claims w hich had not paid a
shilling profit to “investors.” Not only is it totally
beside the question (o vindicate the artificial and dis-
honest inflation of a profitable mine because other mines
have been unlucky (!), but Parson Morris is confounding
“gamblers” with “ investors.”
* ft *
Newton and Rossiter, the only two survivors of
the While Rock, have again made a start with that awful
example of N.S.‘Wales’ silver mania. Mine and machinery
on which tens of thousands have been spent stand them in
at £OOOO only ; hence, if the mine will not pay at that it
must he an everlasting duffer. Concentration of very
low-grade ore is the difficulty. The specific gravity of
the mineral is such as to render effective concentration
almost impossible. With a chloridising plant on the
ground to reduce the concentrates to bullion, additional
vanners and the erection of the remaining 40 heads now
on the ground they would probably make it pay, but
without these additions we are very much afraid of it.
# * *
A 120-acre block of land is, we are told,
held under gold-mining leases, by one man, on the
Kooltahookra goldfield, N.S. W. No work of any kind
lias, it is stated, been done upon these leaseholds for
some years, and at least 100 acres of them have never yet
been tickled with a pick. Adverse applications by
persons willing to test the ground have resulted in
noth ng further than an apparently approving inspection
by the Warden’s bailiff. The holding potentate’s exclu-
sive rigid, to the ground remains impregnable, and, with-
out employing labour, he may snugly hold on to
privileged possession until an adjoining” claim strikes
something rich and makes his “ property ’’—comprehen-
sive term—worth market manipulation.
ft ft #
During the Charters Towers boom seats were
bought on the C.T. Exchange for 250 guineas ; £lO cash
would secure one now. The said boom was engineered by
an enterpiising individual who had (or thought he had)
the entire confidence of one of the local banks, and tilings
were very well indeed until a word came along from the
head-office.
ft ft *
It is time some restrictions were placed
upon that glaring bane to mining enterprise paid-
up shares. Paid-up interest is invariably held by
the “first robbers,” and the delightful privilege which
immunity from call-paying gives promoters to hang on
indefinitely has led to the undoing of many a genuine
nvestor. The holder of paid-up shares is usually “in the
know',” andean play a waiting game, and starve out the
honest and trusting and paying shareholder, should there
be secret proofs that there is a good thing on. There
w ould be fewer wild-cat ventures boomed with a blaze of
big names if there were no such tilings as paid-up shares
—except to bona-fide owners. Paid-up shares usually
mean Swindle.
