T. H. Williams, M L. A., for Upper Hunter,
lias given notice in Parliament of his intention to move
that the whole of N.S.W. be proclaimed a goldfield.
This, with a free-and-easy Mining on Private Property Act
(when the latter is passed) should satisfy the most
ravenous prospector.
ft ft *
The Victory mine, Charters Towers, lias declared
its 32nd dividend. The hope of striking the Brilliant reef
was long entertained by shareholders, but the event was
so delayed that thousands of shares wore sold by followers
of the axiom that anticipation is always greater than
realization—true in too many cases, but not so in this.
So far as developments and ernshings have gone, the
Brilliant reef has increased in lichncss in the Victory
ground, where it is averaging five to six ozs. to the ton,
and returning lens of thousands in dividends. It has
saved more than one Sydney man from bankruptcy.
ft ft ft
The Opliir mine, Croydon, Q., which recently
returned a 33000 z. cake, is worked by a party of six
trihutors, who have another twelve months to.-run. We
can quite believe that they will work (lie mine for all it is
worth, and not forget to pray for rain during the coming
v ar. Croydon is a drought-stricken country, with
precious little water-catchment when rain does fall.
# # *
Tim working of a rich tribute is often an exciting
opera! ion when the end N hear. One at Gvmpie had
several local-preachers among the shareholders, who,
under ordinary circumstances, would not work on the
Lord's Day for any consideration. Their tribute expired
at twelve o’clock on Sunday night, ami on that, as well as
on several previous Sundays, they worked as hard, os they
knew how, and all the stone they could not crush they
look good care to remove from the mine, in compliance
with (he conditions of the tribute. On Bendigo, we have
seen six stiifts of men in a good tribute helling away for
their very lives, and paid £2 10s. for 24 hours’ work a
week.
# # ft
What has become of the Nambncca Heads
mine? asks a correspondent. It has reached that
quiescent stage so characteristic of N.S.W. goldmines.
Two years back it came before the public in occa-
sional newspaper paragraphs re an immense and rich
pyrites gold-hearing body. Treatment was the only diffi-
culty. It generally is in N.S.W. Parcels of a few tons ot
ore were sent to Ballarat, Clyde, and elsewhere, and
these returned many ozs. to tlie ton. When the writer
was asked what he thought of these wonderful returns he
simply advised the owners to send hundreds and
thousands of tons in the same direction if they had it.
The mine is not far from a shipping port. Ei entually, at
a cost of some thousands, chlorination-works were estab-
lished. Then came the little, very little, share boom.
Tiie crop ot fools is perennial; and so many people
believed the newspapers and bought shares up to 355.
Finally came the return of some 2 ozs. to the ton, when
fully five times the yield was anticipated. Now, every-
thing is as silent as the grave, and shaves are offered at
7s. Cd., with apparently few, if any, buyers at any price.
# # »
The “ Wild Cat ” man is cordially urged to come
to the Swamp Oak field by a correspondent whose letter
clearly indicates him a pnactical miner. The field is
described as a good little field, but the tremendous
parentheses with which that “ little” is enveloped indi-
cate that between his and the opinion expressed in these
columns about the field there is no great gulf. Our
correspondent speaks favourably ot the Highland Mary,
Victory, Rainbow, Rising Sun and Great Britain, and says
the claims are mostly held by poor men, which accounts
for their being worked on a small scale. The reason we
supply—it is because the curse of the New England “ Wild
Cats” is upon it, to wit—Enmore Consols, Bear Hill,
Hopetoun and “ pups,” Butcher’s Reef, Castlerag and
“ pups,” Webb’s, White Rock, Red Rock, and a string of
others.
ft ft ft
At last the Pambula newspaper correspondent
has issued a wail concerning that frosty field. He
deplores a rapidly-diminished population, the desertion of
tiie mines, even the sly-grog shanties have not survived.
Less than 100 wages-men are employed, and the place is
again almost a howling wilderness. Of course lie puts it
all down to bad management. They generally do in
N.S.W. But our experience has been that a rich and
permanent field will “boss” the worst management. It
requires precious little management, good or bad, to
delve out rich ore if it’s there. Pamhula’s trouble is
simply the absence of sufficient gold. Eight months ago
Pambula prospects were here sized up and the writer was
laughed at as a pessimist. Time has brought shares down
to zero ; absolutely worthless blocks have been deserted,
and a sincere pity for the deluded local cheese-and-hacon
raisers forbids the enjoyment of a little laugh ourselves.
Another attempt is being made to form an
electric power and water company at Hillgrovo, N.S.W.
At one time it was proposed to bring water from the Gara
River, at another to generate electrical power there. This
was followed by others to utilise the tremendous falls at
Wollomomhi. Each scheme was very practicable, but they
all had one great drawback—want of money. It is now
proposed to conserve the water at the head of Baker’s
Creek Gorge—another feasible project, provided the pro-
moters have enough coin.
* * ft
At present it looks as if the rich run of gold
worked near the surface of the Sunlight mine had dipped
into the adjoining property, that of the West Sunlight,
whose ten-head battery is nearly ready to start on a fairly
large body of stone, estimated to average about an ounce
to the ton.
ft * *
Balstrnps, like the rest of the Tasmanian silver-
mines, appears to have almost fizzled out. If ever a pro-
perty had a chance to assert itself, this had. Taken in
hand by the Broken Hill Proprietary following, led into
it by Bowes Kelly, it had a tremendous working capital,
which has been expended in thoroughly prospecting the
lodes in every direction. True, the Manganese Hill lias
all the external characteristics of the big .Barrier mine,
with one exception-comparative absence of silver.
* * #
A gloomy description of “Zeehan in 1892”
appears in the Mining Standard. The population is set
down at 5000, the hotels described as numerous ; new
railways are spoken of, and everything accomplished is
said to be the work of a twelvemonth. The last is the
biggest cram of the lot. The writer was in the district
over IS months ago when the boom was at a maddening
pitch. The field was then a year old, the Zeehan railway
was in course nf construction, and the population greater
than now. To talk in the strain of this correspondent of
a Held writhing in insolvency is to wear the belt as an
optimist but to sit at the bottom of the class of truth.
* # #
Speaking of the Hargreaves mine, tbo Austra-
lian Star, innocently or otherwise, says that an inspec-
tion of the ore-will be convincing proof of the “ richness of
the property ’ the inspection of a man with a heavy
15-carat gold chain would lie convincing proof of his
wealth. The Bulletin trusts that Hie richness of Har-
greaves mine will exceed the hopes of its most sanguine
shareholders, and it will bo mighty rich to do that.
That, however, has yet to be proved, and the finding of
a bonanza on a field famous or infamous for its patchy
nature is nothing to “ gas ” about. We compliment the
shareholders on the pluck displayed in the working of
this mine, for which it is to he hoped they will receive
just and generous reward ; but the writer would remark
that legitimate mining operations and the frequent
exhibitions of specimens seldom run in harness.

“When anything came before the board of
directors in which its chairman, Sir M. 11. Davies, was
interested, he withdrew from the board-room, and the
fact of his departure was carefully recorded in the minute-
hook.” This interesting it m appears in the report of the
Colonial Investment and Agency Co. of Melbourne. All
the same, the Colonial I. and A. Co. burst in the usual
hopeless manner, and it burst because of the money
which it advanced, presumably, when Davies was out of
the room, to companies in which Davies was largely
interested. Which suggests one of two things-either
that stern Roman K.C.M.G. should have stayed in the
board-room, or else that the other directors should have
cleared out along with him.
A GREAT LIBRARY.
DYMOCK TO THE FRONT AGAIN.
Seldom, if ever, have the book-collectors of
Sydney had a better chance of adding to tiieir
library than that at present afforded by Mr.
Dymock, at his well-known Book Arcade, George-
street. This magnificently-arranged establish-
ment, at any time, is well worth a visit, but just
now additional interest attaches to it, as there
is on view the whole of the late Mr. Street’s
library, which is, without doubt, the finest collec-
tion ever offered for sale in the Australian. So im-
portant was this collection considered by the
executors that they had almost decided to send it
to London for disposal when Mr. Dymock, with
his well-known pluck, stepped in and made a
handsome offer, which was accepted. To attempt
to describe the library would require the services
of a Quaritoh or a Bohn, but the writer being
neither of these gentlemen, his readers will have
to be satisfied with a rough description.
First and most important Come the Australian
books. This branch is rich with works relative
to the early history of Australia and is considered
by Mr. Dymock to be the most complete he
has met during the whole of his business
experiences. On the next dounter we find a
huge assortment of biographical works, which,
we were told, contains over 1500 volumes.
Close by we sa w the laughable sketches of Leech,
Cruikshank, Caldecott, llawlinson, and the dash-
ing drawings of Gilray, Hogarth, &c. A little
further we come to a complete set of Bewick’s
works. In the art books wo found all the masters
thoroughly represented. To enumerate the stock
would take up too much space, but to a casual
visitor the works are shelved too crampcdly to
give an adequate idea of their value. As
the library has taken over 40 years to collect,
it seems a pity that it should now be broken up,
but any gentleman who is desirous of adding to his
library will now have the one chance of a lifetime.
Mr. Dymock has already been honoured by a
visit from his Excellency Lord Jersey, the Mayor
of Sydney, and a number of the leading citizens,
who all expressed their surprise at the magnitude
of the library, his Excellency in particular being
panegyrical as to its quality. In conclusion, we
would strongly advise all to take the really
generous offer of the proprietor to inspect his large
and varied stock.

The Swing.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
the west coast of Maoriland, the country
■" 11 forest. The trees are tall and fine and
18 • d and numerous long, rope-like vines cling
Val 'nanv of them. The boys are very fond of
tO -"o-mcr upon these, Nature’s ropes, and this
sW " Pi shy was strong in our district.
1 T a tall pine on the crest of a ridge, we direct
on r attention. A lovely vine made a perfect
0 The miners had worked the ground right
the spot where the soil met the Old-Man
U P f t he top of the hill. The slope was thus
t rifio a consequence, when you were out in
VI air the deepening distance was wont to send
1111 ■[ c l ’ eX iilting thrill of danger and freedom and
'Vehement through one’s veins. It is astonishing
, tenacious man becomes when he wants to.
This is perhaps the reason why millionaires hang
on so desperately.
One evening three or four lads were there. One
ized the rope, between hands and legs, and
swim" far out over the gully. If the vine had
loosened then, he would have been fired into
boundless space. As it was, he was returning
f fly at full speed. As his foot touched the
Vrth the rope, half-way up, struck with some
newer against an old, withered limb, and broke it
short off. It fell point downwards, right on the
child’s fair head.
We carried him to his mother. The blood was
flowing from mouth and ears and eyes and nose.
Shortly after midnight the poor boy died, without
regaining consciousness.
We did not go to the funeral. We considered
that our presence on such an occasion would only
increase our despair. Neither did we send an
empty carriage. More, we sat upon a large stump
and swore only to go to the funerals of our
enemies. If this principle wereg'enerally adopted
how large would be the attendance at the grave of
some statesmen when they shunt off ! Methinks
Victor Hugo, with his millions of workmen,
w, ul Ibe distanced. And" the variety would be
ch r ning. Six at the funeral—only six enemies !

IT WAS TOO CHEAP.
A good story is told of a physician, who, on
meeting a patient, put the question to him,
“Well, how did the prescription work?” “Oh, ’
replied the erstwhile invalid, “it was a complete
disappointment.” “Indeed; how is that?’
queried the puzzled medico. “ Well, it cost me
seven and sixpence to have it made up by the
chemist, and here I am better after taking a
couple of doses; all that money has gone for
nothing.” This incident has its ludicrous side,
although after all it is but another verification of
the truth of the proverb that “That which is easily
gained is little valued.” It is a remarkable fact
that some people are so constituted that they can-
not bring themselves to believe that under proper
conditions there is nothing antagonistic between
cheapness combined with simplicity and genuine
merit. The fallacy of concluding, without other
sufficient reason, that because an article is cheap
it must necessarily be proportionately “nasty”
and of small worth, is principally shared by the
ladies, although it is a characteristic of some of
the sterner sex as well. Especially is this the case
with persons afflicted by accident or disease.
They somehow get the notion into their heads
that their greatest safety rests in retaining the
services of a surgeon or a so-called specialist (who
as likely as not will consult his own pocket in not
effecting too speedy a cure), and will scout with
derision the idea of a simple natural remedy being
able to effect any good. To a large extent the
common prejudice against anything that bears the
name of a patent medicine is responsible for this ;
the very important fact being overlooked that
there are patent medicines and patent medicines.
“By their fruits ye shall know them ;” that is
the only legitimate test. The good fame and
known value, for instance, of that greatest of all
household remedies, St. Jacobs Oil, has now
become so widespread and generally recognised in
every clime and country under the sun that it has
in the case of many thousands come to be regarded
with even greater trust than the family physician.
Indeed, in not a few instances, where the best
efforts of the latter functionary resulted in failure,
St. Jacobs Oil has come to the rescue and raised
the patient, crippled and racked with pain, from
a bed of suffering from whiph formerly there
seemed no escape short of death itself.

A writer in Longman’s depicts a lepe r
island in North Australia somewhere. The firs*'
inhabitant was an Englishman who lived alone
till “Providence gave him for companionship
another victim of the same fell distemper, a
Chinaman coolie.’’ He has it all upside-down
though. It was Providence who gave the China-
man coolie the Englishman for companionship.
This is the query per-
What Is petually on your little
boy’s lips. And he is
It For? no worse than the big-
ger, older, balder-head-
ed boys. Life is an interrogation
point. “What is it for?” we con-
tinually cry from the cradle to the
grave. So with this little introduc-
tory sermon we turn and ask: “What
is August Fuower for ?’ ’ As easily
answered as asked : It is for Dys-
pepsia. It is a special remedy for
the Stomach and Liver. Nothing
more than this; but this brimful.
We believe August Flower cures
DyspepsiaT We know it will. We
have reasons for knowing it. Twenty
years ago it started in a small country
town. To-day it has an honored
place in every city and country store,
possesses one of the largest manu-
facturing plants in the country and
sells every where. Why is this? The
reason is as simple as a child’s
thought. It is honest, does one
thing, and does it right along—it
cures Dyspepsia. ®

GALA’S MONSTER CONSULTATIONS.
MODE OF DR AAV INO FOR 100,000 TICKETS
Five barrels are placed on a revolving cylinder. In each barrel ten marbles are placed, numbered
oto 9. A person stationed in front of each barrel simultaneously draws a marble, which is shown to the
scrutineers. The numbers in the barrels represent as under, and in the same order
Ist barrel. 2nd barrel. 3rd barrel. 4th barrel. sth barrel
Tens of
Thousands
Thousands.
fd
Tens.
Units.
To show that the low numbers have exactly the same chance as the high ones, take, for example,
Ticket numbered 7. From the first barrel a 0 must be picked, from the second a 0, from the third aO,
from the fourth aO, and from the fifth a 7. At the first glance it might appear that the four O’s have a
less chance of being drawn than any other number, say 78,088, but this is not so, as to pick this number
you must draw from the first barrel a 7, from the second an 8, from the third a 0, from the fourth an 8.
and from the fifth an 8.
There being but ten marbles in each barrel, marked 0 to !), the chances are just as mud: that you will
pick an oasa 7 or any other number, as the proportion against any individual number is 9to 1. This
rule applies to each barrel. When the horses are being drawn for, a separate barrel is added. The small
number ot marbles in each barrel permits them to be thoroughly and easily shaken up, which cannot be
done when 100,00 Q marbles are together. • ■ -
The systemprevents any possible chance of fraud and collusion, as ten marbles can be easily seen and
counted by all present. It is distinctly understood that should a number be drawn more than once for
the same prize the number can only l.e counted once, but each ticket, has one chance in every prize. The
ticket numbered 100,000 is not issued, but is replaced by a ticket numbered 0.
When a drawing of less than 100,000 takes place the marbles in the first barrel are proportionately
.tduced in number The drawing takes place in the presence ot the press and of subscribers.

"Tattersall’s” Sweeps.
(BY A SUBSCBIBER.)
Tattersall” issued his first sweep— looo sub-
scribers at £1 each—on the Melbourne Cup of 1878. Since
then the business has grown so prodigiously that four
sweeps were issued on last year’s Melbourne Cup—viz., a
100,000 sweep at £1 per ticket, two 50,000 sweeps at 10s.,
and a fourth sweep at 10s., which closed with 36,000 sub-
scribers. In all 236,000 subscribers, representing a sum of
£168,000 ! The largest syndicate application was for 600
£1 tickets ; over 2000 applications came in one morning’s
mail. Since October, 1890, over £300,000 have been paid
to prize-winners for placed horses alone ! “ Tattersall’s ”
commission is 10 per cent, of the total. Get a world-wide
reputation for honesty and business capacity and start as
a sweep-promoter.
“ Tattersall’s ” expenses are tremendous, but his is a
cash concern. Unclaimed money is paid to those entitled
to it. Thus, should a prize fall to a dead man, it is
claimable by and will be paid to the heir-at-law. It is
estimated that “ Tattersall’s ” immense business brings
a revenue of £lB,OOO per annum to Sydney General Post-
olfice.
* * *
“ Tatt.” is just one man. Any subscriber who
can produce a decent reference may arrange to meet him,
and may investigate their joint business concerns, and
may attend a prize-drawing. Of course, no one sub-
scriber can get information about any other—the business
is a confidential one. “Tatt.” tells us that, notwith-
standing all assertions to the contrary, he (unless
authorised in writing to do so) never informs a horse-
owner or anyone else Of the drawer*of a horse in a sweep
until after the race. He is the common enemy of the
Australasian bookmakers, and had his reputation a flaw
they would have extinguished him long since. He hasn’t
drawn a prize in his own sweeps, and only once has an em-
ployb of his done so—for £lOO, some three years ago.
When authorised by a horse-drawer to hedge, or to lay a
horse-owner money to nothing, “Tattersall ” does so. In
the last Melbourne Cup, Sir William was the sole horse so
laid against. Most horse-owners accept the offer, but Mr.
Donald Wallace, Carbine’s owner, always curtly refused :
“ I run Carbine and all my horses to win.”
* * *
Over eight-tenths of Tattersall’s business is done
with people outside N.S.W. Victoria, Maoriiand, Queens-
land head the list. Rut there are numerous clients in
S. A., W.A., the United Kingdom (particularly in London,
Liverpool and Manchester), in South Africa, China, India,
Japan, Straits Settlements, New Caledonia, the South Sea
Islands, in British Columbia and San Francisco ; and
although State lotteries are common enough on the con-
tinent of Europe, there are numbers of German, French
and Italian patrons. John L. Toole, the actor, is a con-
stant English customer.
Christians, Heathens, Pagans, Jews, Buddhists, Mahom-
medans—people of all creeds and colours subscribe.
Not long ago a Sydney Presbyterian clergyman (under
a pseudonym) drew a £lOOO prize. “Scottish, ye ken.”
Tommy, a Japanese, of Thursday Island, drew £22,500
for first horse in the big Melbourne Cup Sweep of 1890.
Tuck Guy, of Toowoomba (Q.), drew first horse (£1800) in
the March Handicap of ’9l; Ah Kong (Stanth'orpe, Q.)
drew second horse (£2430) in the Carrington Stakes this
year. Just Chinamen’s luck ! Sixteen prizes, including
a first, second and third horse,’ have gone to the town of
Bingera, N.S.W., within the last four years. Mr. W.
Rider, of Grafton, N.S.W., drew second horse (£2700) in
the Melbourne Cup of ’9l, and first horse (1048) in the
Canterbury Handicap of January last. Mr. J. Preacher,
East Richmond, Vic., drew first horse (£27,000) in the big
Melbourne Cup Sweep of ’9l. Mr. R. Garlick, Auckland,
N.Z., drew first horse (£8100) in the Carrington Stakes last
January.
* # •
Eight ladies each drew a placed horse last year.
One lady, whose husband forbade her to invest, and
refused to give her 10s. to do so, defied hubby, borrowed
a half-sov. from a sympathetic neighbour, and raked in a
prize of several thousands. Disobedient wife ! One
widow, who won a big prize, wrote thus—“l say thanks
to Tattersall. Gentlemen, the money has come to the
bank. Bless the good horse 1 I was a poor widow woman
with a boy and two girls. I am very thankful.” A Sydney
shoeblack drew first horse (£900) in the sweep on the
Warwick Farm August Handicap, run on Saturdav, 13th
ult.
# • *
Tattersall advertises in all the leading Austra-
lasian newspapers, and a typographical error in Tim
Bulletin advt. of the Melbourne Cup sweeps of ’9l
afforded a peculiarly striking criterion of the circulation
of this journal—especially when it is remembered that
notone-fifth of Tattersall’s business is done with N.S.W.
The 100,000 sweep at £1 per ticket was wrongly advc rtised
in Tub Bulletin as a 100,000 sweep at 10s. per ticket.
Thousands of applications for 10s. tickets in the 100,000
sweep came from all parts of Australia and Maoriiand.
Some incorrect newspaper advt. was manifestly respon-
sible, and, accordingly, the advertising columns of all the
leading Australasian papers were carefully scrutinised.
Titff Bulletin was the sole delinquent.
• * •
Tattersall files and keeps all correspondence.
The ticket-books are on the plan of a cheque-book. He
keeps the butt of every ticket, issued ; on the butt is
recorded the name of the ticket-holder and the ledger
reference. Thus, should a prize-winner lose his ticket his
identity could be proved by his own correspondence and
Tattersall’s records, and the prize would be paid him.
Tatt’s business-system is so simple, and straight, and
complete, that, up to date, no mistake has been debited
against him. Every result-letter despatched tc a patron
has a sign or a number on some part of the envelope. The
sign or the number denotes the race—for instance, this
year’s Mel. Cup sign is a tick, V. The position of the
sign or the number identifies the clerk who despatched
the letter. Foimerly initial letters were used—M.C. for
Mel. Cup ; N.H. for Newmarket Handicap. This arrange-
ment was discarded, for, on one occasion, a disappointed
subscriber to a Newmarket Handicap, wrote, after learn-
ing the result of the drawing, as follows: “You— !
I knew I had no horse when I got my tickets. There was
N.H. standing for No Horse on the envelope they came
in.” Any subscriber may apply for and obtain any un-
issued number in a sweep.” What scope for diviners,
dreamers, inspirationalists, spiritualists, clairvoyants’
witches and wizards ! Often such a letter as this is re-
ceived ; “I want this number very, very, very particular ;
if not in this sweep then in the next.”
* * *
What queer correspondents Tattersall must
have I One woman wrote to this effect: “This is the last
half soV. I have and I really need it to buy bread.” The
money was returned to her—with a ticker., which, how-
ever, brought her no luck. Another woman asked for
£300: "it is a flea-bite to you but a fortune to me.”
Occasionally an imbecile, who would be a rogue, writes
thus; “I forward you money for tickets. Give me a
prize ticket, I’ll refund half and keep mum.” Such an
applicant has his money returned him, with a request to
cease patronage.
* * #
Correspondents write on all subjects either
directly or indirectly connected with racing. Frequent
enquiries are made concerning missing friends, sons,
brothers, fathers, husbands, wives, sisters, daughters,
mothers, and uncles, cousins, aunts, and sweethearts’
who attended any Melbourne Cup or other meeting, or
who held a ticket in a consultation a year or ten years
ago. Doubtless Tattersall has sufficient documentary
evidence to prove that a great many people regard him
ns omniscient and omnipresent. He does his best for all
enquiries. This is oneof the many secrets of his popularity.
« * *
Here is the trick of a “smarty.” He holds ticket
13556 ; 13506 is a winning number. He erases the second
5 on his ticket, substitutes a 0, and sends in the doctored
ticket for the prize. Did he hold the altered ticket up to
the light, he’d burn it rather than send It in. But wily
scoundrels have a subtler scheme for “ getting at” Tatter-
tall and his patrons. Fac-siniiles of Tattersall’s circulars
and pmniphlets are printed and then distributed through-
out Australasia and America, and the tJnitcd Kingdom.
The sole difference between the sham and the real appears
in the name ain't address. Instead of
“ TATTERSALIi,”
Care of George Adams (Owner), Tattersall’s Hotel and
Club, Pitt-street, Sydney,
Impostors have put
GEO. WM. ADAMS,
No. C Tattersall’s Chambers, Hunter-street, Sydney, N.S.W.
Or, Tattersall, care of G. Adams, Box —, G.P.0., Sydney.
Or, Tattersall, care of J. Adams, Box —, G.P.0., Sydney.
During the last three years, five or six such impostors
have traded on “ Tattersall’s ” good name. The Melbourne
Cup is run on the first Tuesday in November, and every
year, for one, two, even four, months before that date,
the impostors are busy.
* * *
As previously stated, any subscriber may attend
a drawing. Numerically Taltersall’s sweeps are five—
-100,000, 50,000,-25,000, 10,000, and 5,000. He has five cor-
responding barrels containing numbered wooden marbles;
that is one barrel containing 100,000 numbered marbles, a
second containing 50,000, and so on. The barrels are
fitted with an internal contrivance which, in the process
of revolution, scatters the marbles in all directions, and
thus thoroughly mixes them. Tattersall merely super-
vises the drawing. The cash prizes are drawn first. Any
subscriber may revolve the number-barrel to his own satis-
faction ; any volunteer may draw the marbles. The
drawer is so placed that he cannot see into the
barrel. The barrel-door is always opened by
the press representatives. The prize numbers are
always Checked by the pressnien and the sub-
scribers present. The pressmen take their list,
Tattersall his, Next, TattersalTs list is checked and
Signed by the pressmen and subscribers. When the draw-
ing for Cash prizes is finished, the marbles are returned to
the barrel and it is locked. Now comes the drawing for
horses. Marbles bearing the horses’ names are first
arranged 6n a tray for inspection, and put into a small
barrel which is then locked. The barrels are placed at
either end of a long table, and any subscriber may revolve
either barrel or take the office of drawer at either. The
pressmen unlock the barrel-doors, and the drawers draw
simultaneously; one Calls the number, the other the
horse. The pressmen and subscribers check both. The
pressmen enter the result on their lists. TattersalTs list
is checked and signed by the pressmen and subscribers as
before. Anyone may take a list. When a sweep, say, of
50,000 closes with 40,000 subscribers, any number over
40,000, drawn, counts, of course, as a blank or no draw.
After the drawing is completed, anyone may examine the
barrels and count and sort the marbles. For this job, a
contractor would be supplied with trays, each honey-
combed for 100 marbles, and numbered consecutively in
hundreds from Ito 100,000. Thus is worked Australia’s
mammoth and world-famous sweep.

The Argus is always quoting Hancock’s
“all things are possible in a Democracy” as a
highly original utterance. Oliver Wendell
Holmes, however, got a long start of Johnny in
the aphorism, which is in the “Autocrat of the
Breakfast Table,” Breakfast No. 5. The Autocrat
says :
As the “O” revealed Giotto —as the one word “ moi ”
betrayed the Stratford-atte-Bowe-taught Anglais—so all
a man’s antecedents and possibilities are summed up in a
single utterance which gives at once the gauge of his
education and his mental organisation. “Possibilities,
sir?” said the dhinity student; “Can a man who says
Haow? arrive at distinction?” “Sir,” I replied, “In a
Republic all things are possible.”

What folly it would be to cut grass with a pair of scissors! Yet peo-
ple do equally silly things every day. Modern progress has grown up
from the hooked sickle to the swinging scythe and thence to the lawn
mower. So don’t use scissors!
But do you use SAPOLIO ? If you don’t you are as much behind the
age as if you cut grass with a dinner knife. Once there were no soaps.
Then one soap served all purposes. Now the sensible folks use one soap
in the toilet, another in the tub, one soap in the stables, and SAPOLIO
tor all scouring and house-cleaning.

Drink.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Drunk, as usual ! —’ere Jemima,
Ketch ’im by the legs ;
Let’s be off before the p’lioeman
Twigs us. Stir yer pegs 1”
That’s the Way they treats the drunkard
When they’ve drawed ’is blood
Chucks ’im through the door and leaves ’im
Sprawlin’ in the mud !
What care they for wife and child’ert
Starvin, like enough ;
Achin’ hearts and bitter mis’ry ?
Curse their blasted stuff !
Here they lift him up between them—
Work for women meet!—
Lift him up and slowly stagger
Down the dirty street.
“ Father, but I’m orful tired,
Can’t you walk it now ?”
Then the answer, short and savage—
“ ’Old yer blinded row !”
Then the creaking door that opens
Shows a squalid room—
Two small children in the background—
Shuts and all is gloom.
Daylight, breaking o’er the city,
Strikes a dusty pane ;
Strikes a sleeping face, forever
Stamped with stamp of Cain.
Two small children in the corner
Cower and sob and stare ;
Stare at something lying loosely
Near a broken chair.
“ Father done it!” so they whisper,
Pointing to the place
Where, beyond all recognition,
Lies a battered face —
Face whereon the hand of murder
Hath large letters writ—
Done to death by him who sleeps now
In his drunken fit.
“ Father dune it.” He must suffer ;
“ Life fur life,” men say :
As in ages past they said it,
So they say to-day !
W.A.A.
Upper Derwent, Tasmania.
Resurgemus.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Although your smile is ever swift for me,
Your words are kind, your actions fond and free,
I know they lack what your true love would see.
Could / but that love he !
Alas ! your thoughts forever inward turn
To where, within your steadfast heart, there burn
The embers of a love for which yo i yearn
With life-long constancy.
But I have not lost hope of triumph yet
Although long years have flown since first we met,
In spite of knowing that our ways are set
With much austerity.
For I believe that when this life is past,
And in another sphere our lots are cast,
Our souls shall meet and there unite at last
For all eternity.
M.T.
Sydney Evening News, speaking of a
recent portrait of Sir Henry Parkes, by a local
artist, says: “The expression of the whole face
has been caught in a peculiarly happy way.” The
Bulletin presumes the picture was finished before
the news of Dibbs’ knighthood and the arrival of
the latest little ’Enry,
The glad news comes from Scotland that
the Assembly of the grand, old, cast-iron Free
Church has decided, by a large majority, to
“ relax ” some of the more hide-bound doctrines of
its creed. It appears from the report that the
Church is divided between the “ auld lichts,” who
consider that anyone who smiles after sunset on
Saturday will be eternally lost, and the “new
lichts” who think that a “professing believer”
may even look comparatively happy on Sunday
without serious danger of being struck by light-
ning, provided he repents immediately afterwards.
Scotland, in the days when the present writer
knew it, seemed to consist chiefly of “auld
lichts.” One of his earliest recollections is about
a hideous row between two old women, one of
whom had sold a hen to the other for half-a-crown
on Friday and called round on Saturday for the
money, whereupon the other old woman refused
to pay because the next day was Sunday, and told
her creditor that she “ wunnered ” she didn’t fall
down dead at the front gate.
The Duchess of Albany (hats off!) recently
paid a visit of inspection to a Women’s Home in
one of the London suburbs. The home in question
is a place where “fallen sisters ” are benevolently
taken in, and worked to death from early dawn
till dark at washing, ironing and mangling, with
no wages, and are visited and inspected by hard-
faced gorgons in spectacles, who lecture them off
and on at all hours till they are nearly dead.
Those who don’t fall down exhausted at the
mangle, or run away to escape the slavery and
the lecturing, are finally hired as servants by
Christian females, who give them only half
the usual pay because they are “ fallen.” Mean-
while, the home does the washing at reduced rates,
and thus starves out the professional laundress.
The Duchess, as already mentioned (hats off as
hereinbefore alluded to) visited this place, and
seeing one wretched inmate near! perishing at
the mangle, “ her royal highness immediately
stepped forward, took the handle herself, and
finished the piece of work that was being done,”
thus giving the unhappy creature a minute and a
half in which to straighten her back. The society
paper adds that “it is needless to say this
spontaneous action of the Duchess was greatly
applauded.” Quite needless. “ Her Grace ” turned
that historic mangle for ninety seconds and the
fact is spread all over the world, and the other
woman grinds the horrid implement from dawn
till dark, day after day, and week after week, and
if she holds out and doesn’t “fall” again, and
isn’t talked to death by some pious visitor or
other, she may grind away till she drops at the
handle from sheer old age, and yet she isn’t
applauded—in fact, she is expected to be pro-
foundly grateful. This is a strange world.

Pomeranian dog, the of Lady Kintore, are
inseparable. The dog lies cuddled in his Honor's
lap whenever he drives around in his landau, is as
often as not carried under his arm when he walks,
it attends him on the judicial Bench, and —
whisper ! —lt is even alleged that it si

centuries ago has been disinterred in Sweden.
All that remained was an exceedingly small skull.
It is safe to tell the truth about queens who have
been dead 600 years.

A Miscalculation.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I used to laugh at him. He ivas a comical devil,
and no mistake, and good company, too—could
sing a song or tell a yarn around the camp-fire
with the best of them. N.B. —He didn’t tell this
yarn around the camp-fire.
Jack Silver and I had just boiled the billy, and
were squatting around the hot coals covered with
fish and johnny-cakes—the coals were—ready to
make a start, when he joined us. He came up,
remarked “ Good night,” set his nest of billies
down very carefully, threw his swag down, sat on
it, had a yarn and his tea, and before we turned-in
that night it was settled that he should travel with
us. Our boat was big enough for three, and as he
was a strapping man and seemed to fancy himself
a bit with the oars, we had no objection.
I’m sorry to say this man was a regular sun-
downer. Yes, actually boasted of it and showed
his proofs with pride. The proofs of a sundowner,
he said, were a nest of billies, suit of clothes on
the outside one, 8 straps around Matilda, 23
tucker-bags—he has a name for each one —and a
general knowledge of every boundary-rider within
984 miles of anywhere. One of the bags, the
“Emergency,” is only brought into requisition
when he sees the bump of generosity very largely
developed on the cook’s head. It is hardly worth
while saying that this is a very large bag and
isn’t often used. He said it came in handy in
very dry weather, though. But the sundowner, at
least the voluntary one, is a novelty. I don’t
care what anyone He is a curiosity to men
who have travelled the bush for years.
He could pull a boat though, and if you praised
him he would fairly make her jump out of the
water. He said if he only had a proper boat he
would make us see the backs of our heads turning
some of those sharp bends —had often done it. He
seemed to know every inch of the Murray. The
third day after he joined us he said we ought to
make the Great Northern Bend that day. I think
he said it was the Great Northern ; but, anyhow,
he said it was 12 miles round by the river, and
only half a mile across. We had been told there
was a very large bond in the river, but didn’t
know it was so large as tha*>. Sure enough, that
evening about four o’clock he told us to pull into
the bank where a large gum with some figures on
it hung over the water. “Well, lads,” he said,
“ this is the big bend. I know it by that tree
there. You fellows can go round with the boat,
and I’ll walk across. It's only half a mile through
the scrub there, and I’ll have tea ready and plenty
of wood for the night when you get there.” This
seemed a good idea, so we concluded that, as it
was such a short distance across, we would
leave all the tucker and swags. We planted
them in the scrub, and, after a lot of
growling, he at last consented to make
two trips and carry them over. As we pulled out
from the bank, he sung out, “Now, you chaps,
git a move on you ! You’ll soon do that twelve
mile down stream with an empty boat. I’ll back
a lame bull-ant to keep ahead of you for weeks,
cuttin’ off a bend now and then.”
We took turn about rowing and steering, and
when darkness settled in we began to keep a sharp
lookout on our left for a big fire. We didn’t see
it. Next morning we saw a man fishing, and he
told us there was a bend of that size soihewhero
on the river, but it was a long way down yet.
We never saw that sundowner again, but we
learned that he had sold all the things he couldn’t
carry—such as the gun, ’possum rugs, etc. —to
some teamsters who were camped at a pub. on the
main road, only half-a-mile from where he left us.
How he made that mistake, knowing the river so
well as he did, I never could figure out to this
day.
BOILING BILLY.
An enquiry in the “Missing Friends”
column of an English paper for a man named
Collins has unearthed a curious story of Queens-
land, as it was in the wild and larky past.
Collins, so the legend runs, came out from Eng-
land and started business as a teamster. Sonic
time in 1870 he went up country with his bullock-
dray and a valuable load. He was then a quiet,
sober, intelligent man, with a good bank-balance
and sundry other property. He struck a bush
pub. at an early stage of his journey, and had his
first drink of Queensland rum. After that he
remained in an obfuscated state for a week, and
at the end of that period he made over his waggon
and bullock-team, his load of goods, his bank-
balance, and everything else to the publican, and
two days later he was shunted into a lunatic
asylum. It is presumed that he is either dead or
still a lunatic —anyhow, he has never been heard of
since. Hum was a convincing fluid in those grand
old days, and the liquor-trade had money in it.
The management of a big steamship firm
having received continual complaints concerning
the slothful despatch of their vessels at Newcastle,
N.S. W., one of the firm, a potentate with no
practical experience, proceeded to that port to
investigate the matter. On his arrival, ho found
one of the firm’s vessels discharging heavy bagged
stuff under the superintendence of llarrh, a smart
and capable stevedore. “ Hullo, Harris, what’s
this? Only three bags in a sling. Pooh! pooh !
this won’t do. No wonder the vessels are delayed
hero. Put six bags in a sling.” Looking at his
employer with unconcealed scorn, Harris (an in-
dependent blasphemer of the most atrocious type)
replied : “Do you think I’m a blinging, blanky,
dashed, doggone fool?” “Sir, I don’t know what
to think of yon—only three bags in a sling, and
such disgraceful language, too!” “Look ’ere,
sir,” said Harris, “ you go back to your
sanguinary office, you’re only a carnation fool
at this work.” “Oh! am I? Hey, you down
there ! (to the lumpers in the hold) six bags in the
next sling—double sling them.” Turning to the
donkey-man, Harris said: “When this blanky
son of a gun gives the word to go, just you turn
on steam, pin your lever, and run for your (adjec-
tive) life.” “Go !” said the employer. The driver
turned on steam, pinned his lever, and rushed aft.
The six bags rose slowly to the puffing and trem-
bling of the donkey for about fifteen feet, then tell
thudfully back into the hold, while the engine,
divorced from its anchorings, made a bee-line
heavenwards, hung in the air for a second, then
landed again on the deck with a fearful crash.
“There! didn’t I tell you you were a blanky
fool ?” said Harris, with a horse laugh. “ Well, I
must admit I am,’’replied the chopfallen owner.
There is an unportfolio’d Minister in the
N.S.W. Works Department, of whom Minister
Lyne once enthusiastically said: “If I were a
monarch I would make that man an ambassador.”
The official to whom casual and meagre reference
is made in the press, and who, for diplomatic
reasons, consents to be known as the orderly to
the Minister of Works, is plain Tom Collins. He
is as modest as the violet, as secretive as an oyster,
and as courteous as Grandison. In the public
service he is known as “ the Butler-General,” or
“Minister of Corks.” Physically he is tall and
slender, like Malvolio; much-shaven, with aquiline
features, rubicund complexion, eyes glistening
with benevolence, and a smile warranted to sub-
due even a professional beauty at forty paces.
The oldest inhabitant cannot remember the time
when Old Tom was not associated with Cabinets,
exercising impalpable but potent sway. Some hold
that he is a Mahatma. Since 1851 ho
for loyalty to the Crown, what John P™’
was to Queen Victoria; for
sagacity and tact, what Oliver le J)ain w a °?’
Louis XL, and for real controlling influence wha?
Bismarck was to Octogenarian Bill. Not a ,
remembers Tom as otherwise than patriai-pfli
Abrahamic. If a reporter could go up half ’
century hence on a deputation he would find T a
beaming the same regulation smile, and sHu
speciously affecting to be a servant and not 1
Minister, neither older nor younger. He ' a
splendid specimen of the almost extinct nolhL.Ji
Struldbrug. There are only four others left-om
Dad, Parkes, George Thornton, and old Glad
stone. As his title denotes, his function is to loot
after the bin and the hamper when Ministers sallv
forth to stump the country. Mr. Dibbs often said
in ecstatic admiration : “ Collins always puts one
in good spirits,” his ambiguous way of statintr
that Tom always puts good spirits into one~ 0 r
two, or legion, as the ease may be. Tom ought t
have gone with Sir Dibbs. When Collins accepted
the office of Butler-General he stipulated that his
colleagues should drink under his direction
assuring them that in such case their days would be
long in the land. The understanding has been
shamefully broken, but optimists yet indulge the
hope that politicians may yet awaken to the faith
that a knowledge of the correct brand through the
Minister of Corks alone exalteth a nation. If
Mr. Collins was not born in the purple he
“growed ” into it. His colleagues are scrupulously
polite to him, for they know not how soon the
whirligig of time may find them standing on the
threshold they will have no right to pass un-
announced, delicately combating his bland assur-
ances that “the Minister has just gone out.” As
a bluffer-off of political monstrosities and “boo-
jums,” or freaks of nature, he is peerless. What
is wanted is a Praxiteles to chisel Tom in his great
act of drawing a champagne-cork. He has been
taken at Doughboy Hollow and Mother of Ducks
for the noble Governor himself. When touring
in the direction of the Gorongobelly Caves his
colleagues, fagged by the journey, begged him on
arrival at Jackass Flat to interview the Mayor, and
ascertain the day’s programme. He called among
other places at the public school, where the kids
were rehearsing the Jersey cantata. The dominie
almost prostrated himself with reverence, and
besought “his Excellency’s ” gracious sanction to
give the children a half-holiday. The Butler-
General stood for a moment in uffish thought, and
then wrote on a sheet of foolscap which specified
the boon the magic words, “Approved. T.C.”
And the little dears responded heartily to the
schoolmaster’s cue, “ Three cheers for His
Excellency the Governor !”

Nat Levi burrows and hammers away with
his Melbourne Daily News idea, but has altered
the basis.
The Devonport (Tas.) Town Board collects
some £350 annually in rates, of which over half
is spent in official salaries, rent, &c. Of the rest,
it is proposed to give the chairman a salary of
.£lOO a year, and the munificent balance will be
expended on municipal improvements

Damper V. Bread.
Now, each bushraan listen to me, for I want you on your
oath
Here to judge ’twixt bread and damper (that is, those
who’ve eaten both); r
I’m not writing now of “ Johnnie ’’-cakes or baking-
powder rolls,
But of flour and salt and water that’s been baked amongst
tllG Co?tls
I have eaten just d few mySelt that might be labelled
good, ...
And I’ve eaten some like leather, and I've tasted some like
wood; , . ,
But when taken altogether you can knock me on the head
if I’ve ever seen a damper that was worth a loaf of bread.
I have never met a “ new-chum who had done a little
“ drove ”
Who wasn’t more a bushman than a ten-year station cove,
And to hear him talk of damper and comparing, it to
bread, , " .
Why! you’d fancy one was feather-light, and think the
IcittiGi* lead *
Now, I always like the new-chums, they’re a sotlrce of
constant joy,
From the boss who runs the station to the little mgger-
b°y>
And though they can’t be always new, and mostly learn
to ride,
While they are they drink thin “rails” and chew thin
dough With proper pride;
Now, I don’t dislike a damper (that’s a good one), be it
said
(Though I don’t dislike the damper, yet I much prefer
the bread);
But to make a real'y good one, cOnie, how hi any of you ve
learnt,
Don’t you often find them sodden, With the outside
mostly burnt ?
Yes, to make them’s very easy (that’s to those who ve
never tried):
Mix your salt and flour with water, make your hole and
put inside
On a layer of little ashes, and then cover up with more
(For the earth’s j our baker’s oven) and it hasn t got a
door).
Then you call a little “ smoko ” while you think with
honest pride
How you’ll like jmur maiden damper (that is burning up
inside) ..
When the “post and rails” are floating, and the halt
hour’s up, you go
And rake away the ashes and investigate your dough.
Well, the odds are, if it isn’t burnt, it’s like half-limited
glue; .
Now, I put it to you bushmen, isn’t what I say quite true?
Then, you eat your bit of tucker, draw your bluej’ o er
j our head,
Soon to dream—of stones of damper in a country made
of bread.
TUR PAUPER.
The Coming Time.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
llark ! the vdices of the fuaue are resounding in men’s
souK,
Like the hurtling of wild storm-winds through ihe gnarled
grey forest-holes;
In men’s hearts the ghosts of things to he are dancing
Carina jnulcs—
The time is coining 1
Do yon hear the millions marching to the ruling of the
world?
Do you hear the tramp of squadrons with their battle l
flags unfurled?
Do you hear the shouts of triumph at the hollow heavens
hurled ?
The time is coming !
l)o you hear the, clanking sabres in the silence of the
night?
Do vnit Imar the arnloitr ringing as they gird then! for the
fight?
Do o i,u near the slogan rising from the people in their
might?
The time is coming!
Do you hear the workers rending all the bonds that gold-
greed ties ?
Do you hear the crash and clamour as the lustful Mammon
dies
In the midmost of the market where men’s blood he sells
and buys ?
The time is coming !
■ Yea: a glorious day is coming when the people shall be
free)
By t e rule of Love sole-governed from the centre to the
sea,
When all men each to other shall as true blood-brothers
be:
The time/is coming!
Yea; Freedom is approaching in her proud triumphal car)
And the glory of her presence cleaves the cloud banks
from afar j
’Through the rifts her tadiance lightens like another
Eastern Star:
The time is coming !
From the wilderness of bondage hark the prophets’ rou »
ing cries,
As they preach the new evangel with a god light hi their
eyes;
They are calling for a leader, for a new Christ to arise:
The time is coming !
STEELR GRKY.
The Salvationists of Australia quiver with
the Canadian intelligence, but appear solid, the
only grievance being that “- staff ” officers do not
share the privations of those “irl the field.”-
Commissioner Coombs came from Canada.
Editor Bulletin, — I think the scarcity of
teetotal names among Immortals due to the fact
that teetotallers as a class are nervously feeble,
Unable to graft hard and late as to drink their
half-bottle without getting up with watery eyes
the next morning.—Yours, J. C.
Dear Bulletin^ —You suggest giving tone
to the triangles by adorning it (is it “it?”)
with white shirts, gloves,-&c. The idea.is auda-
cious, and if evet Dame Justice usurps the
legitimate throne of Mammon may possibly come
off. But more'pertinent to to day is that part of
N.S.W. Minister of Justice O’Connor’s draft
anti-larrikin Bill which exempts from bodily
affliction all under 10 or 18 years. To effect
this he repeals the existing provision for
the fleshly woes of those from 10 to 14, who, it
appears, are actually lickable in some way known
probably to Heaven and the man who drew the
clause, for it certainly has been as dead a letter as
the anti-“sweep ” law. This extraordinary pro-
viso of Mr. O. has escaped you. Now, how on
earth is it defensible, even without the frightful
corollary of the increase of convictions in five
years from 20,000 to 40,000, which, of course,
nobody troubles about? Is it not putting the cart
before the horse? or exempting the junior classes
of a school from the cane? ora father laying by the
rod till the age of knickerbockers is past? Surely
Parliament, whatever it may do with the Major
Striping, will never be stupid enough to sanction
this absurd outrage on the order of Nature. The
reach of the Law over the young wants extending,
not restricting. I’ve heard a boy in cool blood tell an
idiotic mother he “ wished he had a stone to break
her legs.” He should have been punishable on
my information —and so in many other cases
where the parental hand is not. I’ve heard a
P.M. ask a woman, whose child (“ infant ” as he
called it) had taken two lollysticks, whether she
wanted a jury. Nor did he remark on the insanity
of the course he was compelled to offer, insanity
paralleled only by the dementia of the statesman
who tells his official offspring he'll warm them
when they grow up.— James Osborne, Harden,
N.S.W.
